Looking to the future, researchers might pro.tably look to conduct more detailed 








investigations within The Coca-Cola Company to explore how sustainability themes are being built into branding strategy. Future research may also explore the challenges and barriers the company faces in looking to drive sustainability agendas through its supply chain. That said, negotiating access to key decision makers within The Coca-Cola Company will probably prove to be a di.cult issue, in part because of potential sensitivities around the issues of materiality and external assurance as mentioned earlier, while also because of issues of commercial con.dentiality surrounding the importance of continuing devel-opment in the company＊s branding strategy. Research may also explore the extent to which the incorporation of sustainability themes within the Coca-Cola brand strike a chord with consumers＊ values and beliefs and if/how that in.uences buying behaviour. Finally, researchers may look to investigate how other companies are looking to embed sustainability into their brands.  



Coke and the environment 
Simon Lockrey, Karli Verghese, Sophie Langley and Linda Brennan 
The Coca-Cola Company (Coke) has a signi.cant history of assessing the packa-ging designed for its products to evaluate the e.ectiveness of the materials used, and their impact both economically and environmentally. This chapter discusses the ways in which Coke has assessed the packaging of its products over time, and the decisions it has made as a result of these assessments. The chapter also explores the tensions that exist between life cycle assessment (LCA) and other organisational imperatives, and what further use of the method at the organisation may involve in the future. The chapter will use Coke＊s PlantBottle as a case study. 
LCA is an approach broadly used by industries to assess the potential environ-mental impact of a product, process, or service over the entire period of its life cycle. To assist with understanding Coke＊s strategy, we will .rst describe the concept of LCA, detailing its development and, more recently, evaluations more explicitly aimed at assessing the environmental impact of a product, process, or service. 
The chapter will then describe the development of this type of assessment at Coke, evaluate the tensions that have been illuminated between environmental and economic concerns as a result of these assessments, and explore some of the issues LCA highlights for Coke＊s partially plant-based PET bottle, PlantBottle. 
Historical overview of life cycle assessment 
Life cycle assessment, or LCA, is a scienti.c method used to determine the potential environmental impacts a product, process, or service will have over the entire period of a life cycle. Life cycle stages of interest include but are not limited to manufacture, use and disposal, raw material production, product transport, and storage. This will depend on the speci.c product and chosen scope of the analysis (Verghese et al. 2012). 
The concept of LCA surfaced as early as the nineteenth century, when economist Patrick Geddes proposed e.ciency improvements to the product life cycle of coal as an energy source (Weidema, 1997). The .rst half of the twentieth century saw ＆life cycle analysis＊ focus primarily on energy balance and technologies, as oil dependence continued to grow, and alternative energy supply systems such as nuclear power developed (Horne et al., 2009). 
The boom in consumer goods manufacture in the .rst half of the twentieth century brought with it an increased demand for fossil fuels (particularly crude oil), resulting in greater volatility in these resource markets. Therefore LCA at this time focussed primarily on energy e.ciency and technology use, in order to optimise the use of valuable commodities such as oil and to explore the potential of newly developed energy sources such as nuclear power (Horne et al., 2009). 
It was not until late in the twentieth century that the LCA methodology and terminology became best practice for environmental evaluation through the ISO 14040 environmental standards series (International Organization for Standardization, 2006a; 2006b). Such advances allowed some measure of comparability between di.erent LCA studies. This opened up the possibility of using LCA results for new purposes, including marketing and demonstrating performance against sustainability targets set by governing bodies. Such uses were in response to growing interest in environmental issues amongst brands, consumers, and policymakers. 
Industries and researchers now assess broader product and service systems with LCA, where there are multicriteria inputs and outputs of anything from packaging, consumer goods, transport, built environments, and supply chains are observed. The investigation of life cycle impact has also expanded from simply measuring energy and material consumption to include other impacts such as water consumption, land use, chemical pollution, and waste produc-tion, among other metrics. 
LCA is also becoming a larger part of the business sustainability decision-making framework, including for product and packaging, with multinationals such as Walmart (Angeles, 2012), Toyota (Early et al., 2009), and Nestl谷 (Schenker et al., 2014) integrating LCA tools within corporate systems. Gov-ernment institutions are now also involved, from the United Nations Envir-onment Programme (UNEP) and the Society for Environmental Toxicology and Chemistry (SETAC) collaborating on life cycle thinking programmes (Kl.p.er, 2006), to the European Union also integrating LCA knowledge into programmes and legislation (Reale et al., 2017). 
The proceeding sections explore how Coke has applied the LCA, tensions that already exist between LCA and the company＊s other organisational imperatives, and the potential further use of the method at Coke in the future. 
How LCA started at Coke 
Coke .rst applied the LCA approach with an initial study undertaken in 1969, where they intended to quantitatively evaluate packaging options and guide decisions such as whether to move from re.llable glass packaging to single use plastic bottles. This study was originally known as a Resource and Environmental Pro.le Analysis 每 REPA (Verghese et al., 2012). The major innovation in this study framework was the recognition and quanti.cation of the relation between material and energy use, and what environmental consequences resulted. 
LCA process value 
With the REPA framework it was possible for Coke to establish complex comparisons of business options with LCA. For example, they could decide whether it would be more e.cient to use the chemical precursors of plastic production as a fuel source for the manufacture of reusable glass bottles, or if the resource use and environmental impact of single use plastic bottles would be preferable given the e.ects of shipping heavier packaging and collection, transport, and cleaning of bottles for re.lling (Hunt & Franklin, 1996; Verghese et al., 2012). The results of this early study were not published, but a summary released in a 1976 issue of Science Magazine shared that one of the interesting .ndings of the Coke study was that plastic＊s reputation as ＆somewhat of an environmental villain＊ was ＆based on misunderstanding＊, and that Coke had ＆gained a ※comfort§ with the idea of switching from glass to plastic bottles＊ (Hunt & Franklin, 1996, p. 5). This ability to compare impacts of di.erent systems with interdependencies between di.erent inputs and outputs is a powerful aspect of LCA as an investigative tool. It has subsequently provided Coke the ability to challenge assumptions and preconceptions about what constitutes an ＆environmentally friendly＊ or ＆economical＊ product, process, or service (Hunt & Franklin, 1996; Verghese et al., 2012). 
Life cycle issues at Coke 
Following the initial application of LCA with the REPA, a number of initia-tives have followed which have relevance to Coke＊s application of life cycle thinking to their corporate practices. Life cycle principles can be found in particularly two of the three pillars of the triple bottom line, being Coke＊s balancing of economic and environmental factors (Slaper & Hall, 2011). They are discussed in the following sections. 
Economic 
Coke has a track record of using environmental actions to also address eco-nomic issues, such as reducing costs or market risks. As one executive notes, ＆We＊re seeing a lot of variance in pricing because the price of petroleum is so volatile＊. Over the long term, this executive believes, ＆the cost of plant-based material will be more stable than the cost of petroleum＊. For this reason, Coke says it has built its supply chain ＆to e.ciently use materials made from plants in our packaging＊ (Moye, 2014). This approach is behind Coke＊s development of the PlantBottle, which uses biopolymers rather than oil-based materials. The PlantBottle, which will be discussed in more detail later in the chapter, is made by converting sugarcane into ethylene glycol for 30 per cent of the total composition of the polymer that the bottle is made of (Niaounakis, 2015). 
When e.ciency measures are implemented with an environmental focus, cost reductions often follow. Coke has demonstrated this through its move to thinner PET polymer bottles due to concerns of overpackaging. A 25 per cent material reduction since 2004 led to annual savings in production and trans-portation of $6 million per year (Walsh & Dowding, 2012). As raw material costs relate directly to product sales, material e.ciency as justi.ed by using LCA can lead to cost e.ciency. 
Cost reductions have also resulted from water saving measures, with more than $1.6 billion saved through water e.ciency in recent times (Rhone, 2016). This is an area where corporate .nancial and environmental interests overlap, which can be useful when organisations look to solve multiple issues with the same solution. However, Coke has recognised that there are also social dimensions to its water policies (Walsh & Dowding, 2012). For instance, Coke run many ＆community projects＊ (mostly in Africa), but somewhat paradoxically the organisation e.ectively controls the water in these regions rather than water control remaining with the local population. Thus tensions between the pillars of the triple bottom line exist, an issue Coke must address with any such multi-pronged measures. This is especially signi.cant as water security becomes a global security priority, and a high percentage of the world＊s population faces ＆extremely high＊ or ＆high＊ levels of baseline water stress, where a high percen-tage of available water is withdrawn from water stocks every year (Willem Hofste et al., 2019). 
Another environmental issue is the premium that consumers are prepared to pay for sustainable products, which remains problematic. Consumers may now have an appetite for change, with a 2015 study .nding that 66 per cent of consumers surveyed were willing to pay more for products and services pur-chased from companies committed to positive social and environmental impact, up from 55 per cent in 2014 and 50 per cent in 2013 (Hacker, 2016). This trend has the potential to shift non-commercially viable sustainable products into being viable to the market. However, there are other consumer studies that counter this trend, such as Deloitte (2009), which suggested there is a gap between the intention to buy green and the purchase itself. This could be an issue for Coke in providing sustainable options that are commercially viable. For instance, cost parity for the PlantBottle compared to standard PET is not yet possible (Moye, 2014), so it remains unclear if customers are willing to pay a premium for such ＆green＊ products. 
Environmental 
Coke has a substantial environmental footprint with complex dynamics. Solid waste, water use, and the impact of PET packaging production all contribute in a variety of ways. 
Solid waste is a highly contentious and visible issue for Coke. The organisa-tion is a major solid global waste polluter and the main waste polluter in America and Asia, due mostly to PET bottles (Neo, 2018). Coke packaging incorporates a high proportion of recyclable materials (Neo, 2018); however, this is only half the battle. No matter how many recyclable materials are included, if they are not being recycled, then the environment su.ers. It is also necessary to consider break-even points for the net bene.t of recycling. LCA is a key tool to ascertain this net bene.t. 
LCA reveals that recycling still consumes many resources with associated impacts 每 in collection, shredding, packaging, and delivery operations. Recy-cling impacts are not entirely recouped by the recycled material, sometimes to the detriment of the process. In a 2009 study, Mexican recycling of PET over 35 per cent by mass was actually more costly to the environment based on these additional recycling operations (Romero-Hern芍ndez et al., 2009). It is clear that a more considered approach to analysing the extent to which recy-cling actually makes sense is necessary. For Coke, LCA could be used to con-sider these associated impacts. 
Coke made a ＆water neutral＊ pledge in 2010 (Walsh & Dowding, 2012). As previously noted, Coke has saved more than $1.6 billion up to 2016 by implementing water e.ciency programmes (Rhone, 2016); however, it is unclear how they are tracking on a water neutral goal, as data on this issue is not publicly available. 
The greatest climate change impact of Coke＊s operations is in PET produc-tion for the material impact of packaging (Romero-Hern芍ndez et al., 2009). This could potentially be reduced by the use of biopolymers (if the source is from a low carbon material) and/or the appropriate level of recycled material which can be determined through an LCA approach. Second highest in climate impacts is the PET blowing to make the bottles 每 an energy consuming process (Romero-Hern芍ndez et al., 2009). Again, e.ciency measures or the use of waste energy could assist in reducing this, too, as veri.ed by LCA. 
Llevot and Meier (2016) highlight the environmental aspect of Coke＊s signi.cant move to biopolymer packaging. Three types of materials can be categorised as biopolymers: polymers made from renewable raw materials (bio-based) that are biodegradable, polymers made from renewable raw materials (bio-based) that are not biodegradable, and polymers made from fossil fuels that are biodegradable (Niaounakis, 2015). Biopolymers are not always biodegrad-able or made from a renewable resource. They are also not always compo-stable, and may require a speci.c process to be broken down (Niaounakis, 2015). This can be somewhat problematic when consumers may expect a bio-polymer to just break down naturally in the biosphere post use. 
Moreover, Llevot and Meier note that ＆renewability does not necessarily correlate with sustainability and the overall process has to be compared and evaluated to judge if a sustainable solution is found＊ (2016, p. 4802). LCA is particularly useful here to compare the credentials of biopolymers to oil-based polymers, including metrics other than just fossil fuel use. Biopolymers may not actually be better for the environment because of the resources used during feedstock production driven by agricultural processes (Hottle et al., 2017). Therefore, clarity about the speci.c life cycle credentials of the biopolymers Coke uses is required or they run the risk of being criticized for not being transparent or speci.c about their environmental strategies. 
As is evident above, there is a complicated tension between economic and environmental pillars of the triple bottom line for Coke. Central for life cycle thinking going forward is the paradox of economic growth, and by reducing environmental impacts for more than e.ciency which, as acknowledged above, can lead to both economic growth and environmental impact reduc-tion. However, there may be a problem in reducing consumption which can lead to decreasing pro.ts. The current state of this economic and environ-mental conundrum is explored in the following section. 
Economic and environmental tensions 
In the e.ciency approach economic and environmental approaches can exist in harmony. However, outside of this approach competing organisational strate-gies can clash. Coke＊s PlantBottle highlights several of these tensions. For instance, Rhone (2016) points out that Coke sold more of the water brand Dasani, after putting it in the PlantBottle. This is counter-productive on environmental and social grounds, especially in the case of water being almost the only beverage in existence that does not need consumer packaging to get to the consumer (in countries with potable water). Therefore, a bottle designed to reduce impacts may instead be increasing impacts with a growth in sales of the brand. LCA can quantify this paradox. 
Additionally, some new Coke packaging lines are described with words such as ＆honest＊ and ＆simply＊. Coca-Cola European Partners＊ Commercial Director Simon Harrison said the British launch of the PlantBottle was ＆consistent with o.ering a wide portfolio of sustainable soft drinks＊ (Selwood, 2016). However, a 30 per cent plant-based bottle is only one step toward a potentially ＆sustainable soft drink＊ and this sort of language is consistent with greenwashing, where vague qualitative descriptions are used rather than clearer objective or numerical data. 
For instance, this language does not provide any insight into the use of water stocks by Coke＊s operations in its production regions. This kind of data is of increasing signi.cance as water availability grows into a global concern. Research from the World Resources Institute found that 17 countries 每 which contain a quarter of the world＊s population 每 face ＆extremely high＊ levels of baseline water stress. With irrigated agriculture, industries and municipalities withdraw more than 80 per cent of their available supply on average every year. Forty-four countries, which contain one-third of the world＊s population, face ＆high＊ levels of stress, with more than 40 per cent of the available supply withdrawn every year. Countries with extremely high or high water stress can still e.ectively secure their water supply, but proper management of this resource is vital (Willem Hofste et al., 2019). 
As Kalkowski (2017) argues, there are other issues in increasing consumption through the PlantBottle, in that bottles being promoted as having been made out of plants (when in fact they aren＊t entirely) could encourage customers to buy more. This type of ＆rebound e.ect＊ is a common paradox of sustainability 每 that interest in the sustainable option actually drives greater impacts through an overall increase in consumption. It is evident that Coke is actively introducing new lines and using the PlantBottle to promote them. For instance, the Minute Maid range was expanded with the introduction of three new .avours: Lemonade with Mint, Spicy Watermelon Lemonade, and Peach with Ginger Juice. PlantBottle packaging was used for these new releases (Beverage Industry, 2017). Using such initiatives to grow the market of envir-onmentally conscious consumers inherently encourages increased consumption (Hacker, 2016). Such an approach could be increasing a particular corporation＊s existing level of consumption, rather than growing markets. Thus Coke actively faces the life cycle paradox of the environmental impact of growth competing with environmentalism, where impacts increase even as the brand communicates e.orts to curb them. 
New horizons 每 Coke and the circular economy with a focus on materials 
The LCA approach highlights the need for a more nuanced, circular approach from Coke to sustainability. This section will discuss some key tenets for future life cycle initiatives for Coke, with a focus on materials, and further explore the PlantBottle as a case study that demonstrates Coke＊s entry into the new context of a circular economy. 
Future life cycle initiatives for Coke should include industrial packaging, unit packaging, and the content itself. This approach would allow Coke to consider the bene.ts of optimising the performance of any packaging versus minimising material use in packaging (Walsh & Dowding, 2012), the embodied energy of packaging materials (Verghese et al., 2012), the energy use associated with the manufacturing use of di.erent materials (Romero-Hern芍ndez et al., 2009), and the relative bene.ts of renewability compared to sustainability (Moye, 2014). Other considerations would include the volume of waste and how that waste is used, either through recycling or repurposing. An assessment of recycling would need to consider trade-o.s in recycling packaging between the bene.ts of recycling materials and the energy required to collect those materials for recycling (Romero-Hern芍ndez et al., 2009). 
The best use of materials requires balancing raw materials and the manu-facturing/use phase with the functional requirements of the packaging. There is a trade-o. between packaging materials as .t-for-purpose food protection and the food product mass or waste without that packaging (Verghese et al., 2015; Williams & Wikstr.m, 2011). As Verghese et al. (2012) note, food and beverage brands tend not to focus on food ingredients in their LCAs, instead honing in on packaging. There is a gap here in Coke＊s assessment of its products＊ environmental impact and on the issues sur-rounding serving sizes. This is problematic as global focus has increased on food waste as a key issue, even in the packaging .eld (Verghese et al., 2015; Williams & Wikstr.m, 2011). 
Coke＊s PlantBottle provides a useful case study to consider the company＊s entry into the circular economy. In the next section, we will explore the ways in which this packaging product highlights both opportunities and challenges for the company in this new context. 
End of life options 每 the PlantBottle 
Coke＊s PlantBottle packaging was launched in 2009 and rolled out to several of its beverage products. As mentioned earlier, PlantBottle uses a material that is made in part with sugarcane residue, rather than petroleum. By 2015, accord-ing to the company, the packaging accounted for 30 per cent of Coke＊s packaging volume in North America, and 7 per cent globally (Moye, 2014). Coke＊s Global Program Director for PlantBottle, Michael Knutzen, said that the ＆breakthrough technology has helped catalyze a moment to redirect the entire polyester .ber and plastic resin industry towards a renewable future＊ (Moye, 2014). 
However, the PlantBottle has faced criticism as an example of ＆green-washing＊; ＆Selling products from renewable constitutes a marketing advantage for the companies, both by making customers less scared about the toxicity of the products and conscious of protecting the environment,＊ according to Llevot and Meier (2016). A bottle made partially from plants may seem to consumers to be less ＆toxic＊ to the environment. Toxicity and sustainability can, of course, be related, but they aren＊t necessarily coupled. Intentional con.ation of these two considerations as ＆green＊ constitutes greenwashing. 
As to whether the technology has proved to be a catalyst for the movement towards more renewable plastics, this remains to be seen. Only Coke has used the PlantBottle commercially thus far, in spite of global issues such as the recycling crisis derived from Chinese policy changes, petrochemical plastic contamination issues, and climate change. Heinz uses a biopolymer in some capacity, too, but it is not yet economically viable (Niaounakis, 2015). It has been suggested that bio-based PET may be used more in the market from about 2020 (Hottle et al., 2017). 
How much is made from plants? 
Coke has also faced criticism over the proportion of the PlantBottle that is, in fact, made from plants. Coke＊s marketing around the packaging product does say it is partially made from plants (Moye, 2014), but critics have argued that this is an exaggeration, as the bottle was found by a Danish consumer protec-tion organisation to contain only 15 per cent plant-based materials. The criti-cism extended to several of Coke＊s marketing approaches, including the use of the word ＆plant＊ in the name, the colour green, and a logo that looks similar to the familiar recycling logo (Hacker, 2016; Zara, 2013a). 
The company responded quickly and publicly to these accusations, with Coke spokesperson Scott Vitters saying that PlantBottle＊s bene.ts 每 including a reduced carbon footprint 每 had been documented through research carried out by third parties (Zara, 2013b). According to Vitters, those third parties included a biologist from Imperial College of London whose team had carried out an initial LCA of the packaging product, and found it reduced the impact of carbon emissions by 12每19 per cent. Vitters also named a researcher from Michigan State University＊s department of chemical engineering and materials science as having ＆touted the product＊s bene.ts＊ (Zara, 2013b). 
The response from Coke, which stressed the involvement of third-party partners and that the marketing ＆positioning＊ that was facing criticism was lim-ited to a speci.c ad campaign, suggests the importance of a product like the PlantBottle for potentially shifting the company＊s broader image to a ＆greener＊ one. This suggests ＆company image＊ could play an important role in creating, and perhaps managing, the tensions between environmental and economic concerns. 
In addition to managing these marketing issues, Coke is also attempting to continue developing biopolymer materials that could allow a greater percentage of its PlantBottle to be made from plants. The PlantBottle currently uses ethylene glycol made from bio-based ethanol. However, this represents only a portion of the .nished packaging, as previously discussed. A xylene derivative, called puri.ed terephthalic acid (PTA) constitutes around 70 per cent of the mass of polyethylene terephthalate (PET) bottles. Coke is collaborating with start-up .rms Avantium, Gevo, and Virent to develop plant-based substitutes for petroleum-based PTA (Tullo, 2016). Gevo makes PTA from biomass using a technology that is ＆easily retro.tted into existing ethanol plants＊ (Niaounakis, 2015, p. 29), which is an advantage as it may reduce the resources needed to .t the technology, meaning uptake of the technology is more accessible than if entirely new facilities were necessary. Another US-based start-up called Anellotech is currently ＆developing a method to convert lignocellulosic biomass into benzene, toluene, and xylene (BTX)＊ using non-food plant feedstock matter. This could be used in the production of PTA. The non-food materials used include ＆palm trunks, sugarcane bagasse, corn stover and pine＊; however, the reactor has not yet been commercialised (Tullo, 2016). 
Issues with plant materials 
Even if more of the PlantBottle were made from plants, there are also impor-tant questions to be considered about the sustainability of the plant material itself. Coke has said the ＆current and future plant sources＊ for PlantBottle ＆do not compete with food crops＊ (Moye, 2014), which presumably could include some of the non-food materials the Anellotech reactor is able to process. Taking the material currently used 每 sugarcane bagasse 每 as an example does allow us to illuminate some of the potential issues and considerations for an expansion of the use of the PlantBottle, using this or any other plant material. 
The use of sugarcane bagasse (or sugarcane residue) has bene.ts, including the capacity for such use to minimise waste in other parts of Coke＊s production line. But it is unclear if there is enough of this material to supply all of Coke＊s needs, especially if it rolls out the PlantBottle to a larger extent, given that sugarcane is not the primary sweetener in some of the company＊s products. 
Linked to this concern are the sustainability issues that surround sugarcane itself, both in its growing phases and in transporting the crop. Cane sugar grown in Australia, for example, carries considerable environmental issues, such as taking up rainforest land and seeing waste .ow out into reef areas (Sager, 1995). As previously mentioned, global water stress is an increasing issue (Willem Hofste et al., 2019), and pollution of potential drinking water with waste .ow could be an issue that increases water stress in some regions of sugarcane production. These questions are key for Coke to consider if it intends to commission local manufacture of the PlantBottle in any region. Additionally, sugarcane is not a viable crop in every region and Coke would need this product to be present in all of its markets if the PlantBottle was fur-ther rolled out. Therefore, the transport of raw or processed materials would need to be considered as a factor in any LCA undertaken for the packaging. 
These issues do lend themselves to a solution that makes use of other plant materials as well. Though for this to succeed, the plant materials would need to be viable for producing both the ethylene glycol component of PET and the PTA component. It remains to be seen whether materials other than sugarcane can be used for the component of the PlantBottle that is currently made from that material. 
Recycling PlantBottles 
Bio-based PET products can be recycled along with conventional PET products using the same techniques (Niaounakis, 2015; Hottle et al., 2017). Indeed, Coke＊s PlantBottle is marketed as a ＆drop-in replacement for its pet-roleum counterpart＊ (Merrington 2017). However, other bio-based plastics are not as easily recyclable. Polylactic acid (PLA) is a bio-sourced and compostable alternative to PET, but contamination by this product is often cited as a major concern for PET recyclers as it is di.cult to separate the two plastics in most recycling facilities (Merrington, 2017). This indicates the potential for confu-sion, and the need for recycling systems to adapt to such fundamental changes in the material they are receiving. 
As discussed earlier, recyclability does not necessarily guarantee that packaging has reduced environmental impact (Romero-Hern芍ndez et al., 2009). Reuse could be a better option than renewable material recycling, from a life cycle perspective, for Coke to tackle the circular economy. However, even this needs thorough life cycle considerations to be viable. Indeed an Italian study noted that reusable bottles for water are a good choice, until the reuse reverse logistics network becomes less spread and transport starts to play more of a role in driving environmental impacts (Nessi et al., 2012). Coke is exploring this tentatively. The Coca-Cola Freestyle 每 a touchscreen-operated beverage dispenser 每 operates with micro-chipped, self-serving reusable cups, mugs, and bottles. The reusable items and returnable technology are designed to provide consumers beverages with a reduced packaging footprint. Whether the equipment, technology, and cups/bottles may break before becoming obsolete is unclear but something an LCA could help to verify. 
Waste in other parts of production 
In addition to the issues noted earlier associated with marketing language likely increasing consumption, focusing on the packaging of Coke＊s products allows other parts of the manufacturing and production phases where waste could be an issue, as well as any food wastes, to remain invisible. These areas are already less visible to consumers, as the packaging that is left over is really the only part of the entire manufacturing process that is easily visible to them. This approach is further problematic, as ＆studying the dynamic between packaging and food is more relevant as it has been determined that the energy embodied in food is signi.cantly higher than the energy embodied in the packaging system＊ (Verghese et al., 2012). 
Packaging is estimated to account for an average of only 10 per cent of the total energy inputs for one person＊s weekly consumption of food 每 and it ＆plays a critical role in ensuring that the other 90 per cent of energy inputs to the supply chain are not wasted＊ (Verghese et al., 2015). Williams and Wikstr.m argue that ＆there is no doubt that the environmental impact can be signi.cantly reduced if food losses decrease＊ (2011, p. 48). They argue there is a need for analysis of packaging design beyond its material ＆sustainability＊, instead analysing any packaging design changes for whether they pose any risk of increased food losses, which would also increase the overall environmental impact of the food or beverage product (2011). 
It is clear that ＆product protection needs to be the primary goal for packaging sustainability＊ and that this will sometimes require ＆trade-o.s between the amount of packaging and the amount of food waste that is generated＊ (Verghese et al., 2015, p. 604). There are several factors for Coke to consider in order to reduce food loss or waste. For instance, while the bottle part of the PlantBottle packaging is currently made partly from a biopolymer, the lid and seal are made from conventional polymer. As Coke continues to develop the design of this packaging, it needs to consider whether biopolymers perform as well to keep its products adequately sealed to prevent both leakage and bev-erage deterioration. Serving size is another potential trade-o. between food waste and packaging volume. Smaller serving sizes may mean a greater volume of packaging per serve, but where there is a trend for households to be smaller and household members are looking for more convenience and pre-prepared foods, the potential for food waste could be reduced by avoiding leftovers that may not keep well (Verghese et al., 2015; Williams & Wikstr.m, 2011). Related to this is the capacity for packaging design to include information, such as storage instructions and assistance in interpreting best-before dates, etc. for consumers to help address food waste (Williams & Wikstr.m, 2011). In addition, the bottle itself is only one part of the packaging across the life of the product in the supply chain. A focus on the PlantBottle also allows the impact of sec-ondary packaging 每 additional layers to protect and contain the primary packs during distribution 每 to remain invisible and therefore not analysed. 
＆Packaging that is altered in order to reduce food losses can lessen the total environmental impact and lead to large gains＊, write Williams and Wikstr.m, ＆even if it is necessary to increase the environmental impact of the packaging itself＊ (2011, p. 47). It is important to understand more fully the impact of food or beverage losses in order to understand how packaging might reduce the overall impact of that food or beverage item. Williams and Wikstr.m discuss the di.erence between a food which has a high environmental impact, such as animal-based cheese, and a product that has a lower impact, such as vegetable-based ketchup. Food losses for a cheese product would have a higher impact than losses for ketchup. This might mean a food company could increase the impact of its packaging for cheese in order to reduce food losses and therefore overall environmental impact, where the lower ratio between the impact of ketchup production and its total environmental impact might mean a focus on reducing the impact of packaging would be more helpful (2011). To more accurately understand and address the environmental impact of its products, Coke would need its LCA to consider the environmental impact of agriculture, food processing, retail, consumer storing, and transports for its beverages. 
Final thoughts 
In this chapter, we have discussed the ways in which Coke＊s PlantBottle, and the marketing around the packaging product, have e.ectively rendered invi-sible several key gaps in the product: the proportion of the packaging that is made from plant matter, the sustainability of that plant matter, and the poten-tial impact on current recycling practices of such a material. LCA illuminates these gaps. 
We have shown how Coke has faced criticism in this respect, and been accused of ＆greenwashing＊. The company clearly places importance on being seen to be ＆green＊, responding publicly to this criticism with an attempt to maintain its credibility in this regard. At the very least, this suggests that sus-tainability has considerable marketing value for Coke, which, optimistically, shows an opportunity for pushing the company to continue to make changes that make its production and products more environmentally friendly. How-ever, the exchange of criticism and defence discussed in this chapter also hints at key tensions between Coke＊s economic and environmental goals. 
We also discussed some key economic and environmental considerations, and the potential for tension between them, which LCA helps to identify and expand upon in Coke＊s approach to sustainability, in particular with packaging. Economic e.ciency bene.ts include the consideration that plant-based mate-rial could be more cost stable over time compared to petroleum-based products and that material and water reduction for sustainability reasons also sees a reduction in costs for the company. However, a potential economic issue is whether consumers are willing to pay more for a sustainable product, which, especially in the development stages, cost more than conventional packaging products to manufacture. This begins to show some of the tensions that arise between economic and environmental concerns for a company such as Coke, where more environmentally sustainable packaging may reduce some costs while increasing others. 
LCA reveals key environmental considerations. We highlighted the fact that there is still a range of issues with packaging if it needs to be recycled, including recycling uptake and the energy consumption and resources associated with recycling. Another key environmental concern that LCA illuminates is the impact of the production of PET materials, although these may be mitigated somewhat by Coke＊s move toward biopolymers. 
The chapter also discussed how this move further highlights the tensions that can arise between economic and environmental considerations. LCA makes clear the paradox of growth and environmental impact reduction, where the economic imperative of increasing growth is directly at odds with the environmental concern of reducing consumption. As well as this, the chapter showed how LCA can reveal the ways in which Coke＊s use of the PlantBottle to promote new beverage lines inherently increases consumption, which is unlikely to be limited to increasing a company＊s share of an existing market. 
The context and application of LCA has changed considerably since Coke＊s original study in 1969. Where the original study, conducted in the context of a global energy crisis, focused on the relative e.ciency in energy use of using single use petrochemical-based plastics instead of re.llable glass bottles, current and future LCA needs to consider a more complex array of issues. Coke＊s current actions are moving towards renewable raw materials, recyclable packa-ging options, a circular production model, and issues relating to the role of packaging in food wastage. 
As previously shown, Coke＊s approach has been to develop the PlantBottle as a packaging product that is made partially from renewable materials. This is the approach it looks likely to continue to follow, at least in the immediate future, collaborating with biopolymer start-ups to increase the proportion of its PET bottles that are made from renewable sources. However, LCA illuminates several key issues with this approach that should be considered. Coke will need to assess the sustainability of the plant crops it plans to use, both in terms of the environmental impact the growing of that crop has in any given region, and (where it is not viable to grow the crop) the impact of transporting the raw materials to other markets where the company wants to use it. There are also potential issues for recyclability with the components of PET bioplastic made from newer plant materials that will need to be tested. This may also require a shift in conventional recycling practices, as recycling systems adapt their process to accommodate the new materials. 
Such a strong focus on packaging can, inadvertently or otherwise, work to keep the impact of other production processes invisible. LCA could help fur-ther illuminate other environmental considerations for Coke＊s beverage pro-ducts such as food wastage. There is also potential for the role of packaging in helping reduce said food wastage to be considered. 
As we have seen, there are many signi.cant issues to consider for a company making changes to its product or operations to be ＆more sustainable＊. Coke has pledged to take various di.erent actions, and set a range of targets. However, global environmental activism organisation Greenpeace has said Coke＊s targets are not high enough (＆Coca-Cola pledges to recycle all packaging by 2030＊ (BBC News, 2018)). Another issue with the proliferation of claims, pledges, and changes to graphic design is that they have not yet been followed up with any complex reporting of outcomes. As we discussed, Coke has reported on some of the .nancial outcomes of reducing material and water use in its pro-duction lines, but there has been little said about the impact of these reductions beyond the company＊s bottom line. 
This example highlights several problems with self-prescribed and reported targets, initiatives, and plans: the quality of data and motivation of a com-pany reporting on itself may be questionable and there is a lack of compar-able metrics between di.erent companies. For instance, it is unclear whether Coke＊s water reductions include embodied water, as well as the physical input and output from the manufacture of the .nal product. This lack of clarity and comparable metrics, as well as a general lack of consensus about what sustainability measures should include and what their exact meaning is, can mean that a company essentially sets a target and then .ndsa wayto report this target as having been achieved, regardless of what has actually happened. Consequently, it remains unclear how much real di.erence has been made. 
There are organisations, such as B Lab, that o.er certi.cations (Hacker, 2016). Some, including B Lab, aim to create some consistency in claiming sustainable practices across di.erent companies and industries. This is a good start, but the issue remains that losing a certi.cation is not strong enough a punishment for failing to meet the standards, and the certifying organisations have no ＆teeth＊ to take further action on a company＊s performance. For example, Newsweek ratings of ＆green＊ companies have Coke ranked ninth in 2016, alongside an article about the company＊s initiatives, but by 2017 the company had dropped to 300th (Rhone, 2016). This illustrates how little a rating can mean to a large corporation and what little consequence there is for non-action or performance on life cycle impacts. It is also possible for a com-pany to shop around for a certi.cation that highlights its good performance on particular metrics 每 or even a single metric 每 if it has been found to fall short on others. 
It is clear that the accountability process for corporations ostensibly acting for the greater social or environmental good doubles as a marketing tool 每 as we have seen with the signi.cant marketing bene.ts Coke＊s PlantBottle has created 




for the company. The issue here is that this means there is no incentive for real transparency without actual regulatory involvement. Centrally, for its sustainable e.orts to be truly signi.cant, Coke must focus on initiatives that make a substantial change as veri.ed by LCA, and be clear on what strategies it is applying. For example, it should be clear whether the company is focused on reuse, recycling, renewability, or all of them 每 on cli-mate change reduction, water reduction, or both, etc. Although there have been good initiatives implemented by Coke, the paradox of pro.t over planet has still remained a vexed and complex conundrum when applying such strategies. 











10 Coke and the Cold War 
Laureen Kuo 
Coca-Cola occupied an important place at the core of American social activity and was a ＆taste of home＊ American soldiers overseas craved in the midst of World War Two (Pendergrast, 2013, pp. 14, 56, 58, 164, 184每95; Allen, 1995, pp. 255每8; Watters, 1978, pp. 173每4). For Americans, Coca-Cola not only incarnated ＆the pause that refreshes＊ (1929 slogan) but also embodied ＆the sub-limated essence of America＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 9) and perfectly represented a symbol of the United States and the American way of life (Pells, 1998, p. 199; Allen, 1995, pp. 251, 258; Roger, 1996, p. 274; Rigoulot, 2004, p. 41; Rubin & Rubin, 2004, p. 145; Costigliola, 1992, p. 77). In the post-World War Two world, Coca-Cola was more than just a drink 每 it was a symbol of a new order. While the United States of America had played an instrumental role in freeing Western Europe from tyranny, many were concerned with the way that the Americans were capitalising on their wartime victory. Coca-Cola＊s rapid entry into Western Europe in the aftermath of World War Two was a case in point. Coke prompted particular concern in France, where the soft drink emerged as something of a lightning rod for fears about American hegemony on the one hand and concerns about France＊s declining power in the international arena on the other. By examining Coca-Cola＊s struggle to gain a foothold in the post-war French market, this chapter demonstrates the degree to which Coke＊s global imperative could be challenged by local forces. Coca-Cola＊s struggles in post-war France also provide an important reminder that its global expansion was never guaranteed, let alone seamless. 
*** 
Coca-Cola＊s global expansion was already beginning to accelerate in the inter-war period. By the end of the 1920s, Coca-Cola had their own bottling plants in 27 countries, except for Europe (Pendergrast, 2013, pp.158每60). One of the principal objectives of the company was to enable Coca-Cola to be found, as the 1927 slogan went, ＆Around the Corner from Everywhere＊. 
After the failure of its ＆conquest＊ in the Old Continent in the 1920s, Coca-Cola returned to Europe by reaching out to European markets in the immediate post-World War Two period. However, its successful experience for more than half a century in the New Continent could not be successfully replicated in the Old Continent. In Belgium, Italy, Poland, Switzerland, and Austria, Coca-Cola was opposed and attacked by Europeans, particularly by leftists. At the time, none of the European countries had experienced an anti-Coke tide as violent as that in France (Kuisel, 1991, pp. 99每100; Louis & Yazijian, 1980, pp. 76每7). Coca-Cola＊s arrival in France stirred considerable controversy and provoked intense opposition among French wine producers, other beverage interests, French communists, leftist intellectuals, and the media. In addition to the media attack, Coca-Cola experienced a succession of administrative obstructions, lawsuits, and diplomatic run-arounds and incited a trade con.ict between the United States and France. 
Coca-Cola＊s history in France can be traced to the beginning of the twen-tieth century. For the .rst time, Coca-Cola followed the advance of American troops into France. French businessman Georges Delcroix introduced this ＆national drink＊ to Bordeaux to satisfy American soldiers＊ longing for their hometown beverage. When US troops returned home, Georges Delcroix switched his focus to the French market and began selling Coke to caf谷s in Paris and other major cities, but sales were disappointing. With the exception of American tourists, few French people were interested in the ＆caramel-coloured syrup＊ (Watters, 1978, pp. 179每80, 184; Kuisel, 1991, p. 100; Pendergrast, 2013, p. 157; Kahn, 1960, p. 27; Bureau d＊谷tude, 1949). In 1930, The Coca-Cola Export Corporation established the Soci谷t谷 Fran.aise des Breuvages Naturels, a French plant that was charged with bottling and selling in France, but which was dissolved by the end of 1945. In the following year, The Coca-Cola Export Corporation planned to expand its business into France and other European countries by establishing an o.ce, the Bureau d＊谷tude (Bureau d＊谷tude, 1949; De Teyssier, 1949; M谷morandum, 1947). The ＆wher-ever-at-whatever-the-cost＊ policy of Robert W. Woodru. echoed the maps plastered on the walls of the company＊s headquarters on which Coca-Cola charted its ambitious post-war expansion plan for entering the European and North African markets. 
However, Coca-Cola＊s 1947 investment project, which aimed to establish a concentrate production and bottling factory, was rejected by the French gov-ernment. In the following year, with the authorisation of the French Ministry of Industry and the Bouches-du-Rh.ne (Mouths of the Rh.ne) Department, Coca-Cola established a branch in Marseille to produce the drink. Paris knew that, as a legal matter, it was impossible to prohibit Coca-Cola from establishing a branch in France. However, the rejection of Coca-Cola＊s secret formula import request and, consequently, the Marseille plant＊s venture could be com-pletely blocked. After the import request was rejected, Coca-Cola bypassed the French government＊s obstructionism by importing the secret formula from its plant in Morocco (Guindey, 1949; Minist豕re des Finances, 1949; Secr谷tariat d＊Etat aux Finances, 1950a; Secr谷tariat d＊Etat aux Finances, 1950b; Direction des Finances Ext谷rieurs, 1950a; Bureau d＊谷tude, 1949). However, Paris refused to let the matter go and began attacking Coca-Cola on the legislative and jurisdictional battle.elds. The French Ministry of Public Health and Ministry of Agriculture approached the issue from the angle of public hygiene by investi-gating Coca-Cola＊s ＆7X＊ formula to determine whether it was harmful to public health. Meanwhile, Coca-Cola encountered obstructions in legislation from three opposition forces. The .rst came from the Boulet bill that was backed by French winegrowers＊ interests, MRP 每 Le Mouvement R谷publicain Populaire 每 and many other deputies from the rural constituencies (D谷put谷 Boulet, 1949). This legal project, which was introduced into the National Assembly by Deputy Paul Boulet, adopted a disguised form to legislate against Coca-Cola. Further opposition came from communist deputies who proposed a legal project to completely prohibit all operations and importation of Coca-Cola in France and in all countries of the French Union (Groupe communiste, 1949). Another legal project, the Thibault bill, which was as destructive as the Boulet bill＊s disguised ban and the communists＊ immediate outright ban, pro-posed the prohibition of any beverages made with therapeutic extract, whether synthetic or vegetal (D谷put谷 Thibault, 1949). 
Coca-Cola executives were extremely anxious about these legal projects. They initially approached the French government about the matter through diplomatic channels. Then-vice president of The Coca-Cola Export Corpora-tion, John Talley, immediately went to the US State Department to resolve the Boulet bill problem. Alexander Makinsky, the European representative of the Coca-Cola Export Corporation, promptly mobilised his MRP and RPF 每 Le Rassemblement du Peuple Fran.ais 每 networks and went to the American Embassy in Paris and the Economic Cooperation Administration (ECA) to see whether the US government could play a part in the matter. The US Under-secretary of State, James E. Webb, dispatched the American ambassador in Paris, David Bruce, to lobby the French prime minister, where the bill was temporarily halted (Acheson, 1949; Timmons, 1949; Allen, 1995, p. 9; Reymond, 2006, pp. 261每2; Le Monde, 1949b). However, Coca-Cola＊s trou-bles had not yet ended. In early 1950, a series of legal tests con.rmed the presence of phosphoric acid in Coca-Cola. Paris immediately accused the chief executive o.cer of Coca-Cola of violating French law. At the same time, the Boulet bill was once again introduced into the National Assembly and passed by an overwhelming majority. Shortly thereafter, the Marseille plant＊s permit for the import of Coca-Cola＊s formula from the Moroccan plant was sus-pended. Coca-Cola executives visited the Ministry of Finance to attempt to resolve the import permit suspension problem. Aside from a perfunctory response from French government o.cials, nothing changed (Le Monde, 1949b; Secr谷tariat d＊Etat aux Finances, 1950a; Direction des Finances Ext谷rieurs, 1950a; Direction des Finances Ext谷rieurs, 1950b). 
To resolve Coca-Cola＊s issues in France, the company dispatched their attorney, Stephen P. Ladas, to Paris to help them with their predicament. Pope Brock, Coca-Cola＊s general counsel, instructed Ladas to do whatever was necessary to win the war in France at any cost, stating, ＆This is no penny ante game. It is the decisive struggle in Europe＊ (Allen, 1995, pp. 3每4). Ladas kept Brock＊s ＆no upper limit on the blank check＊ instruction in mind and was ＆full of a .ghting mood＊ to accomplish his mission. After landing in Paris, he was driven from pillar to post and resorted to every possible means to ＆lobby＊ the French leaders of various beverage industries, politicians, and bureaucrats, but was always given the cold shoulder. Ladas again asked the US Embassy to pressure the French government. However, David Bruce refused Ladas＊s demand after considering the sensitive political issues of the time (Allen, 1995, pp. 4每6, 10每11). 
In addition to wine producers and soft drink interests, the main force of the anti-Coke movement included the French Communist Party and intellectuals. Until the late 1950s, the two latter groups were the principal strength of anti-Americanism during the post-war period. The reason for the French wine producers and other beverage companies＊ interests against the entry of Coca-Cola in France was because they were reluctant to see Coca-Cola share the French beverage market (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 222; Kuisel, 1991, p. 102; Kuo, 2017, pp. 117每8; La Journ谷e Vinicole, 1949a; La Journ谷e Vinicole, 1949b). The anti-Coke motives of the French Communist Party and intellectuals were relatively complex and were closely related to France＊s anti-Americanism trend in the Cold War period. 
France has a long history of anti-Americanism. According to Philippe Roger, early in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries, there was already a certain degree of anti-American sentiment in France (Roger, 2004, pp. 10, 22每6, 365每70). Following World War Two, the French Communist Party took the lead in the anti-Americanism movement, both political and cultural, before being replaced by Gaullists in the 1960s (Roger, 2004, pp. 393每4; Duroselle, 1976, pp. 209每12). America＊s predominance in the post-war Western world and its intervention in European a.airs ampli.ed Western Europe＊s ambivalence towards the United States and created growing hos-tility against this superpower. Politically, both leftists and rightists in France in the 1950s expressed scepticism towards the United States. The right regarded Americans as an ally that could not be relied on; the left, in the .erce ideological war throughout the Cold War, continually bashed the ＆imperialism＊ of the United States. Whether right-wing or left-wing, most French people, by disapproving of the idea of an integrated Europe pro-moted by Jean Monnet and the Atlanticists, emphasised that France should take a neutral position and form an independent ＆third force＊ among the two superpowers by escaping from the bipolar US每Soviet confrontation (Katzenstein, 2005, p. 88; Harrison, 1986, pp. 212每3; Judt, 2011, p. 259). Culturally, following the growing American in.uence in Western Europe＊s military and economy under the Cold War circumstances and the imple-mentation of various economic assistance programmes (including the Marshall Plan), American products and mass culture were largely introduced into Europe. For instance, following the Blum-Byrnes agreement of 1946, France reduced its trade barriers to obtain new loans and liquidate its war debts to the United States, a policy that forced France to open its markets to American goods and culture. By the end of the 1950s, a signi.cant number of US multinationals and private investments .owed into Europe following the opening of the European Economic Community (EEC). The ＆invasion＊ of US enterprises and American culture caused disturbances among the Europeans about the danger of the ＆domination＊ of US multinationals in their economies and of the ＆Americanisation＊ of their cultures. This anxiety and resistance was more obvious in France＊s intelligentsia on both the left and the right (Hollander, 1992, pp. 384每6; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 95, 263每5; Pells, 1997, pp. 156, 188每91; Golsan, 2006, p. 45; Harrison, 1986, p. 215; Visson, 1948, p. 207). Everything that represented America could have become a scapegoat for grievances against Washington＊spolicies and ＆American hegemony＊. As Coca-Cola was regarded as the ＆most American thing in America＊, it became a main target of attack in France＊s anti-Americanism movement after World War Two (Wall, 1989, pp. 170每1; Pells, 1997, p. 199; Kahn, 1960, p. 4). 
Sam Dobbs, the sales manager of Coca-Cola, said, ＆Coca-Cola was a bev-erage, but wasn＊t it more than that?＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 100) His statement appears surprisingly appropriate in the anti-Coke context. The attack of the French Communist Party and intellectuals was not aimed at the product itself, but at the political and cultural implications of Coca-Cola. 
Coca-Cola was and remains an excellent model of US multinationals. Their vast global distribution network ensured that Coke was available in every corner of the world. This transnational production and marketing system, in the eyes of communists, was a global expansion mechanism of American ＆degenerate capitalism＊ that would bring nothing but war and poverty (Lazar, 2003, p. 216; Pendergrast, 2013, p. 222; Roger, 1996, p. 260). The interna-tional distribution network could have been a perfect channel for Washington and the CIA to in.ltrate Europe (Rigoulot, 2004, pp. 42每3; Wall, 1989, p. 183; D＊Appollonia, 1991a, p. 147; Kuisel, 1991, p. 101). In the Coca-Cola A.air, French communists portrayed Coca-Cola as a beverage that was harmful to public health and that might have as pernicious addictive e.ects as drugs. They warned that if this ＆American trust＊ was authorised to enter France, it would dominate the French beverage market by using its enormous resources, vast commercial network, and all types of sales promotion devices and consequently would seriously jeopardise the interests of French wine and other beverage pro-ducers (Groupe communiste, 1949; L＊Humanit谷, 1949; Reymond, 2006, p. 271; Rubin & Rubin, 2004, p. 145; Legendre, 1980, p. 240). 
Coca-Cola joined the US Army in World War Two and rapidly spread its brand awareness overseas. Mass advertising and the war transformed Coca-Cola into the symbol of the American way of life (Elmore, 2015, pp. 158每60; Allen, 1995, pp. 250每8; Costigliola, 1992, p. 77; Pells, 1997, p. 199). However, the company＊s worldwide expansion post-war provided indubitable evidence to communists that American capitalism was taking advantage of the war to sweep the world and that the company＊s ambition fully embodied American imperi-alism. The French Communist Party denounced Coca-Cola as the advance guard of US corporate hegemony and the spokesman of capitalism (Baby, 1948, p. 85; Louis & Yazijian, 1980, p. 75; Reymond, 2006, p. 255). Robert 
W. Woodru.＊s statement that every bottle of Coca-Cola contained the ＆essence of capitalism＊ strengthened the connection between Coca-Cola and capitalism (Kuisel, 1991, p. 98; Pendergrast, 2013, p. 227; Louis & Yazijian, 1980, p. 75). The beverage was one of the implements of the Marshall Plan 每 the ＆Trojan horse＊ of American imperialism 每 whose major objective was to economically colonise Europe and to reduce Europe to a subordinate of the United States (De Soria, 1948, p. 83; Brogi, 2011, p. 93; Baby, 1948, pp. 90每2; Roger, 2004, p. 418; Winock, 1975, p. 273; D＊Appollonia, 1991a, pp. 143每4). French communists combined American products with the arguments of ＆cultural invasion＊and ＆economic colonisation＊, assuming that one of the means of American imperialists＊ cultural colonisation policy was to introduce the American way of life to France through multinationals and all types of eco-nomic assistance. By completely ＆Americanising＊ France, France would be economically and politically dependent on Washington and would be entirely colonised by the United States in the cultural and ideological .elds. To most Europeans, particularly leftists, drinking Coca-Cola, buying American cars, and watching Hollywood movies were equivalent to embracing American values and being enslaved by their culture. France had to resist the entry into France of every product that represented the American way of life. Other-wise, France would inevitably be ＆Coca-Colonised＊and become the victim of the American strategy of cultural imperialism (Kroes, 1999, p. 467; Lazar, 2003, p. 220; Pells, 1993, pp. 68每9; Roger, 1996, pp. 273, 278每9; Costi-gliola, 1992, p. 77; Judt, 2011, pp. 200每1; Wall, 1989, p. 181; L＊Humanit谷, 1949; Le Monde, 1949c). 
Instigated by the French Communist Party, the slogan ＆US, Go Home!＊ daubed on walls could be seen everywhere in France. Coca-Cola salesmen were unwelcome everywhere; when they made the French taste Coke, some extremists threw the bottles against the wall. When salesmen went to French factories for Coke tastings, they were insulted and thrown out with all of the cases. French workers indignantly cursed Coca-Cola men, ＆We don＊t want the ※Beaujolais of Texas§＃Americans in America ＃we drink French, we drink wine＊(Reymond, 2006, p. 259). There were brawls against Coca-Cola every-where in France. The company＊s trucks became the principal target of mob attacks. The trucks were overturned, hundreds of Coke cases were smashed and poured into the gutters. 
The French Communist Party, which considered itself the ＆Party of Intelli-gence＊, gathered many intellectuals, writers, and artists together to lead the post-war anti-Americanism movement (D＊Appollonia, 1991b, pp. 11每16, 20; Brogi, 2011, p. 164; Roger, 2004, pp. 555每61; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 81每2, 85; Nettelbeck, 2007, p. 141). The sharp criticism of cultural anti-Americanism after 1945 mainly focused on ＆anti-consumerism＊, ＆anti-mass culture＊,and ＆anti-conformism＊(Winock, 1986, pp. 91每2; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 178, 182每214). Fran-ce＊s denunciation of American culture inherited the viewpoints of extreme right conservatives of the 1930s, which described the United States as a ＆civilisation of money＊ dominated by large capitalist trusts and materialists where super.cial mass culture and conformism were prevalent, an inhuman country where violence and racism were rampant. Although there was no consensus in French society regarding the basis of such radical anti-American arguments, the attack of the French Communist Party against the American way of life caused the most concern for French intellectuals 每 the ＆cultural guardians＊ of the country. The accusations of French communists against capitalism and the invasion of Amer-ican culture helped attract many supporters among the intellectual classes (Visson, 1948, p. 206; Roger, 1996, pp. 260, 282每3; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 182每3; Pells, 1997, 
p. 161; Brogi, 2011, pp. 157每8). French intellectuals were extremely worried about the fact that after World War Two, European markets were dominated by overwhelming American culture and abundant American products. They were unwilling to see American consumerism, fuelled by mass advertising and pro-motional techniques, ＆corrupt＊ European tradition and highbrow culture, and were even more reluctant to see the world-leading position of the resplendent French culture at risk of being replaced by ＆vulgar American mass culture＊ (De Grazia, 2006, p. 78; Kroes, 2006, p. 82; Pells, 1993, p. 77; Pells, 1997, pp. 188, 237每8; Kuisel, 1996, p. 200; Winock, 1986, p. 95). The publications of neutralist intellectuals such as Le Monde, Esprit, L＊Observateur,and Les Temps Modernes, founded by Jean-Paul Sartre, despite their centre-left position on political issues, all aligned with the French Communist Party in the cultural .eld to resist the invasion of American mass culture (Winock, 1975, pp. 271每2; Winock, 1986, p. 90; Rupnik & Humbertjean, 1986, p. 104; Brogi, 2011, pp. 170, 252; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 88每91; Nettelbeck, 2007, p. 139). In his famous L＊Empire am谷ricain, Claude Julien, editor of Le Monde, anxiously warned his compatriots that Amer-icans, backed by their strong economic strength, used all types of formal and informal devices to plant American culture and values in the world for the pur-pose of consolidating their position of cultural imperialism (Julien, 1968, pp. 281每2, 292每309). 
Technophobia, anti-standardisation, anti-uniformity, anti-materialism, anti-consumerism, and anti-conformism has represented the distinct stances of French intelligentsia since the 1930s (Dard, 2008, pp. 115每6, 123每5; Saul, 2002, pp. 257每8; Roger, 2004, pp. 484每95; Judt, 2011, pp. 190每3; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 186每201; Winock, 1986, pp. 92每6). From Georges Duhamel＊s Sc豕nes de la vie future, to Robert Aron and Arnaud Dandieu＊s Le Cancer am谷ricain, these bestsellers .ercely attacked the standardisation of production and consumption mechanisms in the post-war years, which led to the standardisation of person-ality and cultural debasement (Duhamel, 1930; Aron & Dandieu, 2008; Bernanos, 2017). Until the post-World War Two period, French intellectuals were still full of loathing and hostility towards the consumption economy, which was patterned on the American model (De Grazia, 2006, p. 345; Pells, 1997, p. 160; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 81每91; Roger, 2004, pp. 441每2, 527). 
Coca-Cola was a typical representative of a consumption economy (Kuisel, 1991, p. 98). They spent a huge budget every year on advertising 每 a sum Europeans could never have imagined. Coca-Cola used every sort of vehicle for its advertising, such as large lithographs, hand-painted wall signs, news-papers, and TV and radio commercials; Coca-Cola＊s red logo was ubiquitous. The company＊s brochure stated, ＆When you don＊t see a Coca-Cola sign, you have passed the borders of civilisation＊ (Louis & Yazijian, 1980, p. 75). What frustrated the anti-Coke people was that enormous Coca-Cola billboards were erected even in the Sahara Desert (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 232). A series of innovative marketing tactics was used to induce people to consume more. The company dispensed numerous free sample coupons and distributed innumerable promotional items bearing the Coca-Cola script in various forms. Hollywood stars, posters, and calendars that featured sexy ＆Coca-Cola girls＊ incessantly lured people to Coca-Cola (Kuisel, 1991, p. 98; Pendergrast, 2013, pp. 60, 85每6). 
To French communists and intellectuals, businessmen used the incessant hammering of advertisements, all types of commercial methods, and exag-gerated salesmanship to induce people to incessantly purchase what they did not need. Mass advertising, television programming, and broadcasting activities were the best catalysts for conformism. They constantly embedded the common values and moulded people into the ＆standard man＊ who was incap-able of thinking, was enclosed in his own little circle of life, and pursued only material pleasure (Lens, 1959, pp. 388每95; Dard, 2008, p. 124; Hollander, 1992, pp. 49每50; Brogi, 2011, pp. 245每6; Arnavon, 1958, pp. 226每7, 241). American people were as standardised as their assembly line products and di.erence was unacceptable in American society, where uniformity was considered a desir-able characteristic. In addition to material life, Americans＊ tastes, views, and even souls were regulated by the media, advertising, and various conformist devices, and were inconceivably identical (Russell, 1951, pp. 17每18; Domenach, 1960, p. 1523; B谷guin, 1951, pp. 886每8; Lens, 1959, pp. 390每3; Romier, 1927, pp. 85, 135每7, 158; Brogi, 2011, p. 191; Roger, 1996, pp. 278每9; Winock, 1986, p. 94; Visson, 1948, pp. 160每2, 178每9). They were exactly the ＆one-dimensional man＊ described by Herbert Marcuse (Marcuse, 1964). Conformism was no doubt an unavoidable destiny of the American multi-ethnic society built by immigrants from every country in the world (Williams, 1962, pp. 43每4; Domenach, 1960, p. 1524; B谷guin, 1951, p. 886). However, this collective behaviour was completely opposite the values of European elites, for whom ＆distinction＊ was held in high esteem (Maurois, 1956, pp. 317每8; Roger, 2004, pp. 493每5). A statement by Jean-Marie Domenach, the editor of Esprit, perfectly expressed the antipathy towards American conformism where ＆the American state is liberal, but American society is totalitarian; it is possibly the most totalitarian society in the world＊ (Domenach, 1960, p. 1523). 
Americans were not only full of con.dence in their way of life but also intended to introduce their unrestrained consumerism and ＆American uni-formity＊ to Europe. In the eyes of French intellectuals, Coca-Cola was one of the vehicles with which Americans attempted to embed an ＆American model＊ in Europe. This American ＆national drink＊ was more than a thirst-quenching beverage. The 1963 ＆Things Go Better with Coke＊ campaign was an emblem of all the good things in America and a symbol of the American way of life. James Farley, the chairman of the board of The Coca-Cola Export Corpora-tion, proudly assumed with ethnocentric con.dence that this was the ＆only＊ way of life for human beings (Pendergrast, 2013, pp. 12, 221每2, 258每9; Allen, 1995, p. 251). Like many Americans in the post-war years, Farley believed that others equally desired what Americans wanted (McCreary, 1964, p. 130). Coca-Cola proudly noted ＆the almost universal acceptance of the goodness of Coca-Cola ＃ Anything the American .ghting man wanted and enjoyed was something [others] wanted too＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 200). Such ＆missionary imperialism＊ (i.e. transforming Europeans into Americans, teaching Europeans to live well and to think correctly, etc.) not only intensi.ed the post-war anti-Americanism but also transferred this antipathy to Coca-Cola (B谷guin, 1951, p. 884; Roger, 1996, p. 153; Pendergrast, 2013, p. 222). The French newspaper of the intelligentsia, Le Monde, explicitly pointed out that what French people opposed was not Coca-Cola, but the consumption models and way of life the United States, in its political and economic ascendency, transferred to Europe in the post-war years (Le Monde, 1949b). 
To many French intellectuals, American culture was equivalent to mass cul-ture, which was incompatible with the ＆high culture＊ (haute culture) held by the intelligentsia (Guerlain, 1993, p. 199; Chester, 1962, p. 128; Winock, 1982, 
p. 17; Rupnik & Humbertjean, 1986, p. 120). America＊s commercial culture was met with much criticism from communists; Louis Aragon, a leading French communist intellectual, once sneered that America was a ＆civilisation of bathtubs and Frigidaires＊ (Les Lettres Fran.aises, 1951). Andr谷 Malraux, leader of France＊s intellectual community, additionally declared that ＆there is no culture in America that claims to be American. That is an invention of Europeans＊ (Malraux, 1996, p. 181). America only had mass culture 每 press, radio, movies, and advertising. They were ＆psychological techniques＊ designed to persuade people to buy and were the ＆means of propaganda＊ of the American economic system that jeopardised European values (Malraux, 1996, pp. 181, 189). In French intellectuals＊ eyes, the American consumerist mass culture was super.cial, vulgar, commercial, and standardised. Culture was subjectto marketlawsand audience tastes. Itbecame productsfor consump-tion or commodities that allowed merchants of culture to make the most money possible (Pells, 1997, p. 237; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 196每7, 200; Roger, 2004, p. 549; Roger, 1996, p. 275; De Soria, 1948, pp. 128, 133, 194每5). Culture was no longer a public service; all of its precious characteristics, such as quality, exquisiteness, re.nement, taste, distinction, and instruction, eva-porated in lowbrow American mass culture. Daniel Bell noted, ＆the culture was no longer concerned with how to work and achieve, but with how to spend and enjoy＊ (Bell, 1978, p. 70). 
In Ren谷 Etiemble＊s Parlez-vous franglais?, he vehemently denounced the cor-ruption of the elegant French language by English and argued that French culture, from cuisine to ideas, was contaminated by the ＆Coca-Cola civilisation＊ and the ＆American way of not living＊ (Etiemble, 1991 [1964], pp. 283, 377). 
To prevent French culture from reaching a disastrous ※Coca-Colonization§ scenario, General de Gaulle appointed Andr谷 Malraux as France＊s Minister of Cultural A.airs in 1959 and gave him the mission to save French culture from the devastation of American consumerist mass culture (Lacouture, 1976, p. 373; Pells, 1997, p. 238; Brogi, 2011, p. 288; Kuisel, 1996, p. 95). Although some intellectuals, such as Raymond Aron, argued that the consumption society was an unavoidable consequence of a universal transformation of the development of material civilisation, most European intellectuals still linked these phenomena to Americanisation and imputed all the discomforts brought by the Western modernisation process to ＆American cultural imperialism＊ (Aron, 1959, p. 60; Kroes, 2006, p. 82; Bigsby, 1975, p. 6; Pells, 1993, pp. 68, 78, 81; McCreary, 1964, p. 252). 
The intellectuals＊ motive, hidden behind the attacks against American con-sumption and mass culture, was that the coming of a consumption society would deprive European intellectuals of their cultural leadership in Western civilisation (Guerlain, 1993, p. 199; Pells, 1997, pp. 238每9; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 203每4, 211每2; Visson, 1948, p. 39; Winock, 1986, p. 96). Advertising, movies, and tele-vision would replace intellectuals＊ roles as guides for the values and lifestyle of the masses in a rapidly changing modern society. These types of media a.ected people＊s habits and patterns of morals, encouraged prodigality, and emphasised the display of personal achievement and status by all types of consumption (Bell, 1978, pp. 66每9; Pells, 1997, p. 238). To intellectuals, European traditional elite society, dominated by upper-class culture and highly concentrated in the minority classes, would cer-tainly become a ＆collective mediocrity＊ society in which the great mass was the principal customers and the intellectuals would no longer be able to play an important role (McCreary, 1964, p. 253; Roger, 2004, pp. 549每50; Visson, 1948, 
p. 206; Judt, 2011, pp. 202每3; Domenach, 1960, p. 1525). 
Conclusion 
France＊s anti-Coke campaign not only aroused the violent outcry of the US Congress and public opinion but also triggered an overwhelming wave of boycotts against American products in French society. When the French gov-ernment lifted the embargo on Coca-Cola＊s imports and rejected the Boulet bill, Coca-Cola won a victory in France＊s lawsuits. The French parliament＊s exclusionary legislation aimed at Coca-Cola, the Communists and intellectuals＊ anti-Americanism movement, and the political arm-twisting spurred by local beverage interests more or less a.ected the French government＊s decision; however, they were not the key determinants of the a.air, as presumed by traditional views. The reason the French government authorised the entry of Coca-Cola into France was, in fact, based on underlying practical .nancial considerations (Kuo, 2017, pp. 108每45). 
France＊s anti-Coke movement re.ected a complex syndrome caused by huge collective disappointment as a result of France＊s declining power in the international arena. World Wars One and Two led to a shift in world power. The United States de.nitively replaced Europe and emerged as the new great power of the West after World War Two. The situation in the aftermath of the war compelled France to depend on the United States for military and economic support. The feeling of being a protectorate of the United States and recognising that France had been reduced from a tradi-tional great power to a second-class power humiliated and frustrated the French people (Pells, 1997, pp. 158每9; Harrison, 1986, p. 209; Winock, 1982, p. 13; Nettelbeck, 2007, pp. 139每41; Judt, 2011, pp. 195, 256每8). As Bertrand Russell observed, cultural changes were closely linked with the shift of the world＊s great power (Russell, 1951, p. 18). French people, who were always proud of their re.ned culture and regarded themselves as the spokespeople of Western civilisation, found their tradition and culture threatened by the invasion of American mass culture. Europe, a model for Americans to learn from and imitate in the past and America＊s ＆silo of immaterial goods＊, alsolost its leading positioninthe cultural .eld after World War Two (Portes, 2004, p. 277; Roger, 2004, pp. 572每3; Harrison, 1986, p. 210; Pells, 1997, p. 191; Visson, 1948, pp. 27每30, 137; Judt, 2011, pp. 261每2, 273每4; Brogi, 2011, pp. 176, 244). The loss of this power, the disaster and poverty in the post-war years, the humiliation brought by the Lib谷ration, and Washington＊s intervention in Western Europe＊s political, military, and economic a.airs created a collective sentiment of inferiority and resentment against the United States in French society in the immediate post-war period. Given the sharp ideological con.ict throughout the Cold War years, the French Communist Party presented itself as cultural nation-alists and uni.ed the intelligentsia to .ght together against the invasion of ＆American cultural imperialism＊ (Ory & Sirinelli, 2004, pp. 221, 233; D＊Appollonia, 1991b, pp. 12每13; Judt, 2011, pp. 195, 256每8, 280每91; Golsan, 2006, p. 52; Kuisel, 1996, pp. 84每5; Winock, 1986, p. 88). The attack against the emblem of ＆American cultural imperialism＊ waspartof French intellectuals＊ confrontation with capitalism, bourgeois moralism, and the vulgarisation of culture in the anti-Americanism of the 1950s and 1960s. The confrontation began early in the 1930s, and the union of American ＆economic hegemony＊ and ＆cultural imperialism＊ made it even more tumultuous (Mathy, 1993, pp. 161每2; Pells, 1997, p. 200). Coca-Cola would inevitably become the principal target of anti-Americanism because it combined all the characteristics anti-Americanists loathed the most: stan-dardisation, ＆big business＊, capitalism, consumerism, mass culture, the American way of life, and imperialism. Like many di.culties and con.icts encountered by US multinationals in post-war France, the anti-Coke movement was the product of the confrontation of two di.erent ideologies, a fruit of the disturbance and uneasiness created by the upheaval of an old order under the impact of an unprecedented consumption society and a brand new lifestyle, a lament for the loss of the highbrow culture and a manifestation of all the frustrations and discomforts generated by the mod-ernisation process in post-war France.

11 Coke and America 
Vander Casaqui and Adriana Lima de Oliveira 
Introduction 
This chapter examines the historical trajectory and contemporary aspects of Coca-Cola in Latin America, in four of their main local markets: Brazil, Mexico, Colombia, and Argentina.
