According to a recent report from FEMSA (Coca-Cola FEMSA, 2018), the Mexican company that controls most of Coca-Cola＊s operations in this region, these countries comprise 220 million consumers of Coca-Cola products. 
The aim of this chapter is to analyse, in terms of iconography, ideology, and strategy, the meanings that have been created through the brand＊s advertising, and, indeed, to identify those elements that have remained consistent over time. Our study also observes how the relationship between local and global aspects of communication has emerged. To this end, this chapter explores how the image of ＆America＊ is articulated with the brand in its regional expression. We therefore examine how globalisation has been incorporated into Latin American advertisements, and, in turn, the presence of regional and/or local culture in such campaigns. More generally, we also aim to understand how Coca-Cola has e.ectively transcended its status as soft drink .rm to become an icon of consumption and capitalism. 
Our study will focus on the Brazilian experience of Coca-Cola advertise-ments and marketing strategies with a view to o.ering some insights into the Latin American experience more generally. We have selected four moments of the history of Coca-Cola in Latin America. The .rst is the arrival of products and its communication in the region, speci.cally the .rst movements of inter-nationalisation of the brand as well as the impact of World War Two. The second is the post-war era and the role of Coca-Cola in the di.usion of the North American lifestyle and the economic and growing cultural in.uence of the USA. The modernisation of Latin America is based on the consumption society and the disseminations and appropriations of the brand in the counter-culture and local artistic production. Our third period concerns the Coke brand in youth pop culture in the 1980s. The end of dictatorial regimes in Latin America during this decade created new meanings of youthfulness and also attributed new elements for the symbolic universe around Coke. Our .nal period focuses on the globalised culture in the 1990s. In the aftermath of the fall of the Berlin Wall, Coke promoted two simultaneous movements: the intensi.cation of a communication servicing this global imaginary and an aes-thetic of a non-place (Aug谷, 1995), and the occurrence of localised actions that escape the traditional format of advertisement, but ful.l this strategic function. 
The .avour of Coca-Cola is a construct, in.uencing its advertising along with consumer society, which gives meaning to it. In Latin America, the North American brand .rstly had to establish its taste. This meant .nding a place in the daily social and cultural lives of consumers. Coca-Cola consequently invested heavily in advertising and aggressive market strategies. Once inserted into the Latin American culture, Coca-Cola would become a site of appro-priation, resigni.cation, hybridisation, and confrontation. 
Brands, consumption, advertising 
Before we commence our analysis, we must outline the theoretical assumptions underpinning our approach. Firstly, we consider communication to be a social process. To this end, the brand, beyond its market vision, is (as Geertz (1973) notes of culture) immersed in networks of meanings from its time. Being inserted into the Latin American context in the twentieth century, Coca-Cola followed the complex transformation of technologies of communication and local media production processes to establish and cement its symbolic universe in this market. The brand is a social entity, and its symbolism has a close rela-tionship with the spirit of time (Angenot, 2010). The language and imagery used by advertisements consequently re.ect the rhetoric of consumption at that time. When we understand this production, we can infer something about the horizon of the social imaginary of these moments, in which cultural texts are articulated to its historical contexts. 
We view advertising communication as a di.user of contemporary mythol-ogies (Barthes, 1972), which di.ers from the classic mythology due to its ima-gery and aesthetic character. As Barthes says, what is most profound in this culture, which involves entertainment and consumption, is on the surface, in its shape and in its mediatic presence. The mythologies around the brand Coca-Cola are related to what is imagined in the sphere of production, of institutional planning and in its advertisement mediations. These mytholo-gies are also derived from its social circulation; the meanings of the iconic beverage are the basis for new discourses, other ideological positions, and social dialogues. When we refer to consumption and advertising, we are also discussing political, cultural, and historical aspects that are a result of the insertion of Coca-Cola in Latin America. 
In methodological terms, this chapter is informed by the theoretical-metho-dological proposition of Marc Angenot (2010). The concept of social discourse, de.ned by Angenot, considers that the horizon of what is thinkable and utterable in a certain historical movement has an indissociable relationship with the spirit of time. Therefore, the communication of the brand, which is detached from the utilitarian view of the merchandise o.ered and their value of use has, on the other hand, a horizon of ideas and images that characterise its historical moment and that serve as reference for the advertisement rhetoric. What the market calls ＆DNA＊of a brand is something that is preserved in these continuous transformations which communication is subject to, something that is consistently preserved in the communications over time, which also estab-lishes a dialogue between the past and present. 
First moment: Genesis of a relationship 
Coca-Cola commenced distribution and manufacturing in Latin America between the decades of 1920 and 1940. In Brazil, the product began bottling in 1941 with the .rst factory being opened in 1942. Coca-Cola also began operation in Argentina in 1942. Distribution in Mexico and Colombia began earlier, in the 1920s, with factories in both countries being opened over the next decade. These early forays into Latin America re.ected the change in the commercial policy which saw the creation of The Coca-Cola Foreign Service Department in 1926 (subsequently renamed The Coca-Cola Export Corpora-tion (Dinero, 2005) in 1930). Despite having signi.cant success until 1939, the expansionist policy of The Coca-Cola Corporation across the world would escalate when the USA entered World War Two in 1941. 
The impact of World War Two was not only felt in human, geopolitical, and technological terms, but it also had a direct impact on consumption and consumption patterns. Coca-Cola＊s entry into the Brazilian market was under-pinned by the United States＊e.orts to align countries in the American con-tinent with the Allied war e.ort. When Brazil announced that it would be siding with the Allied powers, the US moved to increase the production and commercialisation of Coke in the region. A feature of early wartime Coca-Cola advertisements was an outline of the American continent with the sen-tence ＆United today, united forever＊每a clear demonstration of the ideological role that Coca-Cola was beginning to play. 
Coca-Cola＊se.orts to connect the war e.ort with consumption has been noted. As Hymson a.rms (2011, p. 4), ＆Coca-Cola＊s global expansion ＃ coincided directly with the advances of U.S. imperialism＊. The Coca-Cola Company was not only aligned with the United States＊e.orts to expand its in.uence globally. To this end, it functioned as part of a ＆cultural policy of branding＊strategy, which celebrated and promoted the ＆American way of life＊. Robert W. Woodru. thus ordered the corporation ＆to see that every man in uniform gets a bottle of Coca-Cola for .ve cents, wherever he is and whatever it costs for the company＊(Bodden, 2008, p. 22). Bodden (p. 24) presents the estimate of over .ve billion bottles of soda served to American military men during the war. In 1941, Coca-Cola began manufacturing in Recife, Pernam-buco, to service troops who were located on the bases in Brazilian territory. The next year, the .rst factory constituted by Coca-Cola Brazil began opera-tion in Rio de Janeiro. 
The .rst advertisements and posters for Coca-Cola in Brazil featured the popular iconography of advertisements created for the US market, mainly from the 1920s (i.e. the aesthetic of illustrations of families and North American pin-ups). This illustrative art was transposed for communication for the Brazilian market (Hymson, 2011) and for other countries in Latin America. 
The presence of North American sailors on the military bases along the north-eastern region of Brazil was represented in the brand＊s campaigns. Hymson (2011, p. 133) thus describes a 1944 advertisement: ＆The scene depicts our Brazilian allies on the beach, with the heading ※Esta p＊ra mim (THIS IS FOR ME) ＃ Have a Coke ＃ or how to be buddies in Brazil§＊. The scene shows a common image in North American Coca-Cola ads 每 a woman in a bathing suit. In this example, the female was identi.ed as a Brazilian woman who was admired by two American sailors ＆on shore at Copacabana beach＊. 
Signi.cantly, the depictions of people in Brazilian ads mirrored North American and European conventions (i.e. white women and men) despite the fact that Brazil＊s population was racially diverse. Advertisements published in other Latin American countries similarly featured images of white consumers. Such representations were the .rst form of colonisation of the Latin ＆imaginary＊ in Coca-Cola＊s communication. The images in these campaigns also connect with the semantic .eld of happiness, which would be exhaustively explored in Coca-Cola campaigns in later periods. Happiness would also become a synonym for its .avour. 
Alongside their conventional role of promoting the brand name and selling the product, Coca-Cola＊s advertisements also performed an important pedago-gical function. Coca-Cola advertising had the role of teaching new customers, from di.erent cultures, how to consume. This went beyond the immediate product. Consumers were exposed to a new world that could be obtained through consumption. Coke advertisements not only taught them about desirability of material products such as large, bourgeois houses; they also taught them new ways of receiving guests, along with the North American values concerning family and gender roles. 
As Campos-Toscano and Furquim (2009, p. 66) point out, from the begin-ning of the emergence of industrial capitalism, at the end of the nineteenth century, developed countries ＆began to distribute throughout the world, in a massive manner, not only industrialised products, but their own culture and even other cultures＊. Latin American consumption practices would also be informed by these processes. Featherstone (1991) shows that lifestyle and con-sumption style are interconnected. Through demonstrating the uses of the product, Coca-Cola built prescriptions for a life that was suitable for the cus-toms of their original country. This is evident in another Coke advertisement for the Brazilian market from the beginning of the 1940s. It features an encounter between three young friends. They all have hairstyles that were popular in the United States at this time (which became popular in Hollywood thanks to movie stars such as Veronica Lake and Rita Hayworth), the rosy skin and long-sleeved clothes (which would cause a certain discomfort in some regions of a tropical country like Brazil). The three .gures are depicted sitting by the table, chatting, and eating a club sandwich (an American recipe that was new at the time to the Brazilian market) and drinking Coca-Cola. Under the title ＆A pleasant habit＊, the copy explains: ＆A very cold Coca-Cola has the special gift of making any meeting more amicable. When you＊re with friends, the refreshing break is a pleasant habit with an ice-cold Coca-Cola.＊ 
The advertisement neatly synthesises the di.erent features of Coca-Cola＊s early e.orts to connect with the Brazilian market: a pedagogical tone to teach the ways of drinking the soda; the celebration of the American lifestyle; and the politics of consumption; not to mention the way that the geopolitics of the war invaded the commercial advertisement industry. 
Second moment: The post-war era and the dissemination of the American way of life 
An advertisement published on 6 April 1952 in O Estado de S. Paulo, one of Brazil＊s biggest newspapers, featured the title: ＆All around the world ＃ Coca-Cola is manufactured and sold in over 70 countries, including Brazil, France, Switzerland, Italy, Mexico, Argentina ＃＊ (da Rocha, 2018). The accom-panying image featured stereotypes from some of the listed countries, including the Mexican sombrero and a woman with a fruit hat 每 a reference to the Brazilian singer Carmen Miranda, who had been successful in North American cinema. 
According to Hymson (2011), the post-World War Two era was a pivotal period in Coca-Cola＊s transformation into a corporation with true global range and in.uence. The expansionist North American policy was also associated with a series of profound transformations in Latin American culture. The 1950s saw the arrival of television in di.erent countries in the region. The new medium not only provided a platform for international industries to promote themselves; they also established local operations, which, among other things, stimulated a rural exodus to the city. In addition, the period saw the emergence of a new market with its own popular culture that was geared to consumption. Advertisers understood that this youthful market was not only distinctive, but also lucrative. 
Created in 1963, the ＆Things go better with Coke＊ (＆Tudo vai melhor com Coca-Cola＊ in Brazil) slogan would span most of the decade. The slogan also worked well for television. A 1967 commercial features a group of young people having fun in a pool with Rio de Janeiro＊s famous skyline visible in the background. In Brazil and, indeed, elsewhere in Latin America, this emphasis on youth culture was strongly in.uenced by the countercultural movements in the US. While this movement initially spurned the conventional values and ideas of American culture, it would eventually be appropriated by the very culture it had sought to reject. The incorporation of the counterculture was exempli.ed by the ＆Hilltop＊ commercial. With its famous line ＆I＊d like to buy the world a Coke＊, the commercial was translated into Spanish, and was broadcast in Mexico as well as other Latin American markets (S芍nchez, 2017). The Christmas version of this advertisement, ＆Hilltop Christmas＊ (Organ, nd), was similarly translated and broadcast in Latin America. In many ways, ＆Hilltop＊ foresaw globalisation and multiculturalism and their connection to consump-tion in the 1980s and 1990s. 
Encompassing sex liberation, resistance to authoritarianism, and the violence in civil-military dictatorships that dominated Latin America from the 1960s to the 1980s, the youth market connected culture and politics. The expression of cultural resistance from this period was often appropriated in the icons of the so-called North American ＆imperialism＊, including Coca-Cola. A symbol of the controversial relationship between young Brazilians and Coke can be seen in the artworks produced during the period, which were also in.uenced by the pop art aesthetic emanating from the northern hemisphere. An example of this can be seen in the work by Brazilian artist Cildo Meireles (1948每present). Meireles＊ Insertions into Ideological Circuits: Coca-Cola Project (1970) was produced for a concept art exhibit at MoMA New York and now belongs to the collection at the Tate Modern museum. The work appropriates bottles of Coca-Cola used in the Brazilian market to challenge Coke＊s iconic status. Printed on the bottles were comments of a political character, including ＆Yankees Go Home＊ and the instructions for creating a Molotov cocktail (Manchester, 2006). 
Coca-Cola＊s relationship with criticism, and fascination, meant the brand was consolidated in the minds of Latin American youth. Caetano Veloso＊s ＆Alegria, Alegria＊, from the Tropic芍lia era, thus contained the following lyrics: ＆I drink Coca-Cola / She thinks about marriage / A song comforts me / I＊ll go＊. Tropic芍lia had, as a concept, the idea of anthropophagy, albeit in the cultural sense. While being connected to the global production, it transformed these in.uences into an aesthetic proposition that renewed and a.rmed local cul-ture＊s traditions. In this aspect, Coca-Cola served as an allegory of this youthful attitude toward mass culture and consumer society in consolidation in the country. 
This second moment represented the expansion of meanings of consuming Coca-Cola that were intimately connected to the emerging youth culture (Morin, 2003). The happiness promoted by the brand de.nitively adopted a young ethos, while the complex cultural codes of consumption and the amplitude of discourse saw Coca-Cola inhabit other spaces, which encom-passed the artistic production and the anti-imperialist Latin-American movements. 
Third moment: 1980s pop culture and the spectacle of consumption 
In the 1980s, Hollywood and the American music industry continued to pro-duce and celebrate a global image of youth. As in the previous periods, images and concepts featured in the US advertisements strongly in.uenced the advertisements created in Latin America. Coke＊s advertising during the 1980s expanded the iconography of youth and made a type of globalised language based on American culture. Movies like Fame (1980), Flashdance (1983), and Footloose (1984) dictated the youth rhythm. The American dream was re-edited in a contagious rhythm and in vibrant colours, based on hedonism, on the cult of the body and limitless fun. 
The Hispanic version of the advertisement, ＆Coca-Cola es as赤＊,kept the balance and touch of piano from the original North American campaign. This was paired with local colours and a bucolic landscape, where youngsters, waiting in tra.c, sang together and shared the beverage. There was a sense of universality of what was ＆local＊ (as paradoxical as it might seem). The same campaign serviced markets in Mexico, Chile, and other coun-tries in Latin America. The Korean version, with an identical song and images, only di.ered in terms of the language and the ethnicity of the actors featured in the advertisement. In the 1983 Brazilian version, ＆Coca-Cola 谷 isso a赤 每 Bal谷＊ connected Brazil＊s dance and .tness culture with images referencing the musical .lm Fame. 
The local adaptation of the advertisement reinforces the recognisable pattern of the aforementioned movies and the American advertisements for the brand. This movement of adaptations where global campaigns were localised is something that was in the ＆DNA＊ of the Coke brand 每 long before the term ＆glocal＊ became popular. 
At the same time, Latin icons were being included in these advertising campaigns. One of the best examples was the campaign ＆Waters of March＊ (USA and Brazil with Tom Jobim). In the Bossa Nova era, Jobim was suc-cessful in the USA with the song ＆The Girl from Ipanema＊. In the 1980s, one of his masterpieces, ＆Waters of March＊ (＆芍guas de Mar.o＊), was translated into English and to the rhythm of 1980s pop music, with keyboards, an accelerated tempo, and young people singing vocals. In this version, Brazilian and American cultures were mixed in an expression of ＆hybrid cultures＊ (Canclini, 2005). 
Coca-Cola＊s traditional sponsorship of sports events like the Olympics (since 1928) and the FIFA World Cup (since 1978) generated a myriad of advertising campaigns, both global and local. Such campaigns illustrate the way in which Coca-Cola connects the ideal of happiness with sport. This connection has assumed an even greater importance in recent years in light of the controversies about the harm of excessively consuming soda and the implementation of health policies. By connecting Coke to the festive spirit of these competitions, Coca-Cola sought to embody happiness 每 even if it has had the unintended result of making the brand a target for criticism. 
The controversies around Coke have also provided inspiration for musicians. The rebel discourse of the youth musical movement in Brazil increasingly identi.ed Coke as a symbol of alienation and cultural domination. One of the most successful Brazilian rock songs of that era was called ＆Coca-Cola Genera-tion＊. Composed by Renato Russo, the .rst verses of the song exposed the dissatisfaction of youth (which kept a certain relation with the spirit of ＆Satisfaction＊ by the Rolling Stones): 
When we were born, we were programmed 
To receive what you have been pushing us 
The canned goods of USA, from 9 to 6 
Since we were little, we eat garbage 
Commercial and industrial garbage 
But now it＊s our turn 
Let＊s spit back all the trash 
Re.ecting the restlessness from the punk aesthetic that had infused Brazilian culture after the end of the dictatorial government (1985), ＆Coca-Cola Generation＊ became the anthem of youth over the decade. Ironically, the popularity of this anti-consumerist message would also prove a highly pro.table one for the band. 
In an era of reinterpretations of forms of existence and youth interests, Coca-Cola helped amplify these models globally. Consumer society was deeply in.uenced by contemporary ideas circulating in popular culture. The brand＊s e.orts to connect with sport and exercise, for example, sought to rea.rm Coke＊s association with happiness. However, it also exposed the brand to negative interpretations. Nevertheless, the images of happiness created during this period display a hybridisation between global and local elements, in which the North American culture of the spectacle is the logic that organises the synthesis of everything. 
Fourth moment: Globalised capitalism, social issues and the new logics of happiness 
The fourth and current phase in Coca-Cola＊s history, spanning the 1990s through to the present day, comprises two complementary elements which permeate the brand＊s marketing and communication strategies. However, this globalised ＆New Age＊ also saw the development of new images as well as the transformation of existing ones. These transformations, it should be noted, do not refer to Coca-Cola alone, as they represent the broader global change in consumer society at this time. 
The .rst de.ning element of Coke marketing in this period is the degree to which campaigns were based on the globalised and deterritorialised rhetoric in disconnection with USA icons 每 the ＆nowhere and everywhere land＊ of Coca-Cola. As Hymson summarises, ＆the .ow of global capital, of people, machines, images, and ideas has blurred the lines of a.liation＊ (2011, p. 244). Sometimes, the same campaign changes some actors to establish local connections, but the same images and narrative structures are used across the world. In some coun-tries, local brands were incorporated in Coca-Cola＊s list of products. An example is Inca Kola. Long de.ned as ＆The taste of Peru＊, Inca Kola marketing has maintained its established approach in its marketing communications but the .rm operates as part of a globalised business model. The ＆Share a Coke＊ campaign and its e.orts to personalise consumption by printing Coca-Cola cans with common names from each country (2013每2014) similarly blurred the ＆lines of a.liation＊ while also downplaying the fact that Coke was still a mass-produced product. 
The new era of ＆glocal＊ advertisements sees a mix of global and local images, related to a ＆connected world＊ and a ＆New Era＊. The famous 1993 polar bear campaign, ＆Northern Lights＊, was created during this era. So too was the 2007 ＆Happiness Factory＊ campaign, which featured animated characters and a magical, delirious reading of Coca-Cola＊s production system that was triggered by the consumer placing a coin in a vending machine. Experimenting with the digital technology at the time, the ＆Happiness Factory＊ campaign successfully represented what Jenkins (2006) labelled ＆convergence culture＊, with con-sequences in di.erent media platforms. The campaign rea.rms the possibility of manufacturing happiness and attributes the monopoly of this production to Coca-Cola. In a way, this capacity to commodify happiness is the synthesis of the brand＊s path throughout the years. 
The second de.ning aspect of campaigns from this period concerns local actions that were supported through foundations and institutes of The Coca-Cola Corporation. To this end, the Corporation has sought to legitimise its image by associating itself with various social causes. Such activities have included the support of various NGOs in di.erent Latin American countries, the National Program of Reforestation and Water Harvesting in Mexico, the Water Award in Argentina, and the development of various recycling programmes (＆Un mundo sin res赤duos＊) across the region. 
The de.ning feature of this period was Coca-Cola＊s entry into the culture of entrepreneurship, which corresponds to the logics of a neoliberal society (Dardot and Laval, 2014). In this culture, entrepreneurship and ＆entrepreneur-ship of the self＊ (Foucault, 2008) are considered a panacea 每 a cure-all for the problems and setbacks of our time. This can be seen in the Brazilian project ＆Youth Collective＊. Aimed at lower income youth, the programme of ＆inspiration and counselling＊ sought to coach participants in self-esteem and leadership, as well as training for the job market in Brazil. 
The culture of inspiration 每 which combined the entrepreneurial ethos and the market of counselling, self-help, motivational talks, biographies, and autobiographies of successful businessmen 每 formed the cornerstone of the Youth Collective as well as another initiative of the Coca-Cola Latin Centre. The programme Empreendamos Junt@s Coca-Cola focuses on training and empowering women with small businesses. This project is a part of 5by20, which had the goal of ＆empowering .ve million women by 2020＊. In 2018, seven Latin countries were involved in the programme 每 most from Central America (Nicaragua, Honduras, El Salvador, Costa Rica, and Guatemala) but also Colombia and Ecuador in South America (Coca-Cola Journey, nd). 
The countries that were the target of these initiatives by Coca-Cola had historically struggled with poverty and social inequality. By using these experiences to build narratives of self-improvement into the rhetoric of the Corporation and in the symbolic universe of the Coke brand, the .rm sought to rea.rm its happiness theme. In this narrative, happiness arises from wealth and learning to be an entrepreneur. Although the programmes certainly o.er important and useful lessons, they nevertheless serve ideological functions. To this end, the mercantilisation of the initiative and the discourse of the common good can be seen in the advertising campaign＊s exploitation of life stories and their construction of a saviour narrative. Coca-Cola thus acts as a saviour of humanity＊s need for happiness. A type of ＆new philanthropy＊ outlines a world imagined around the brand. This wonderful, happy world contrasts with the realities of everyday life in these countries, where the majority of people are unsupported by the capitalist system that guides their societies and economies. Coke＊s initiatives in the region demonstrate the degree to which its social activities nevertheless continue to perform a political function and, of course, an economic one. 
Conclusions 
This chapter selected key moments in the history of Coca-Cola to demonstrate the way that the brand has functioned as one of the main di.users of the American way of life in a Latin American context. We have illustrated how the insertion of the brand in the local culture, and its transformations in the world landscape, has led to a contemporary communication that mobilises globalised references, multicultural human representations, deterritorialised images, and spaces. Moving beyond the brand＊s well-known iconographic aspects, we can see the ways that Coca-Cola has actively contributed to the growing in.uence of the American culture across Latin America (and the globe) though its cele-bration and promotion of neoliberal capitalism. 
Rocha (2010), in his study about historical transformations of advertisement in Brazil, identi.es a new capital rhetoric, beginning in the 1990s, based on the logic of neoliberal society. In this spectrum, the corporate agenda of The Coca-Cola Corporation begins to see all their actions as being in the public realm, with the objective of making a more positive image. This is particularly important in light of the criticism directed at it 每 from adverse judicial decisions a.ecting the .rm＊s business practices to the growing chorus of counter-dis-courses condemning the brand 每 spanning anti-capitalist groups, critics of American imperialism, and/or ＆Coca-colonisation＊ (Wagnleitner, 1994), and public health advocates (Campos-Toscano and Furquim, 2009). 
Despite its market dominance, Coca-Cola continues to play close attention to social, cultural, economic, and political issues, and to consider how they need to respond to them. The actions of The Coca-Cola Company in the current context include the incorporation of an entrepreneurial formation, social responsibility, and sustainability as highlighted elements of corporative 


and advertising communication (Casaqui and Riegel, 2016). The corporate reports themselves, including sustainability reports, are accessible to wider audiences through digital technologies. To this end, it could be argued that they function as real pieces of corporate propaganda, with the aim of building, maintaining, and enhancing Coca-Cola＊s symbolic resonance in a changing world. As a part of the Corporation＊s ＆way of going forward＊, it also enables Coca-Cola to rea.rm its role as a manager (or driver) of global happiness, and to ensure that Coke continues to be a desirable commodity both in the present and, indeed, in the future.  










12 Coke and the beach 
Philip Hayward 
As a large, mass-market operation, Coca-Cola has pursued a variety of packaging, advertising, and promotional strategies to establish its brand as an enduring global phenomenon. This chapter addresses a promotional nexus concerning consumer perceptions of the distinctive Coca-Cola bottle as sym-bolising desirable, curvaceous femininity and the related use of beach imagery 每 particularly beachside bodies 每 to associate the brand with summer leisure, pleasure, and desire. While this element constitutes a speci.c strand within overall brand marketing strategies, it has produced an enduring in.ection that has adapted in response to shifting socio-cultural modes. Drawing on branding theory, this chapter explores how marketing has entered into dialogue with social behaviour and popular culture in a process of what might be termed ＆vernacular co-creation＊. As subsequent sections relate, these have proceeded outside of the company＊s proprietary control in manners that variously extend, complicate, and/or problematise its centrally administered brand control. 
The bottle, the shape 
Coca-Cola originated as one of many drinks made and principally retailed by combining a branded type of syrup and carbonated water at (so-called) ＆soda fountains＊ in pharmacies, where such drinks were promoted as refreshing and/ or therapeutic. The production of pre-mixed carbonated drinks in bottles in the 1880s allowed a greater distribution of various proprietary products but also led to issues with product di.erentiation. In warm weather, bottled carbonated drinks were often cooled on melting ice by retailers, leading to the bottle label adhesive being dissolved and detached, making product di.erentiation di.cult. Coca-Cola＊s adoption of a distinct ＆waisted＊ bottle design in 1916 represented a successful attempt to address this issue. The product＊s distinct bottle shape 每 which initially had an even more pronounced narrow central ＆waist＊ than today＊s design 每 merits comment. Coca-Cola＊so.cial web pages identify the contoured shape being designed by The Root Glass Company of Terra Haute in 1915; it went into production in 1916 (Ryan, 2015). The company website identi.es the design has being inspired by the elongated, ribbed, oval form of a cocoa bean and the modi.cation of this for a bottle involved broadening out the tapering lower portion for stability. Despite this botanical inspiration, the bottle＊s contours soon began to be interpreted di.erently, rapidly being characterised as resembling the body of a female wearing a ＆hobble skirt＊ (a fashionable garb ＆where the skirt had a very tapered look and was so narrow below the knees that it ※hobbled§ the wearer＊[ibid.]). As Coca-Cola＊s website adds, it was also called the ＆※Mae West§ bottle after the actress＊s famous cur-vaceous .gure＊ (ibid.). 
Some time prior to the outbreak of World War Two, variations of the term ＆real Coca-Cola＊1 became used in Cuba and other Hispanic Caribbean coun-tries to describe a curvaceous woman, re.ecting the attributes referred to above. This usage played o. the vernacular meaning of ＆cola＊ in Spanish to refer to the (human) derriere and also, allusively, to the ＆tail＊. Like the-appellations ＆hobble skirt＊ and ＆Mae West＊ for the bottle, this Hispanic term represented an early example of an unforeseen vernacular interpretation of the Coca-Cola brand. The latter became so common that several subsequent observers have assumed it was a conscious design strategy on the company＊s part. Interviewed in 1999, for example, the radical Cuban documentary maker Santiago Alvarez contended that: 
The advertisements of capitalism are, in fact, much better than the pro-duct ＃ The Jesuits were their precursors. Remember that saying about giving them a child? Remember too the famous old-style Coca-Cola bottle? Why do you think it was designed like that? Feel it and you will .nd out. It is in the shape of a woman wearing the long dresses of teatime. When a woman has a good body, we say in Cuba that she is Coca-Cola. (Malcolm, 1999, online) 
In recent decades, a similar usage has developed in the English language, with the online Urban Dictionary listing the slang terms ＆Coca-Cola body＊, ＆Coke bottle body＊ and ＆body like a Coke bottle＊ to refer to curvaceous female phy-siques (nd). It is di.cult to ascertain the extent to which the company has deliberately engaged with such popular cultural/vernacular interpretations of the bottle design but 每 as described in the following sections 每 it did not shirk from producing visual advertisements of shapely young women, often repre-sented as disporting themselves on beaches, from the 1930s onwards. The company＊s most overt recognition of the brand＊s vernacular connotations occurred in 2004 in the form of a 46-second Spanish language TV advert shot for the US Hispanic market.2 A group of young Latino men are seen sitting on an inner-city house＊s steps, talking in Spanish to each other in an animated manner when a bus pulls up in front of them. A young, curvaceous Latina woman steps out and walks by them before turning her back to them to cross the street. Falling silent and gazing at her in erotic rapture, one holds a Coca-Cola bottle in his eye-line, juxtaposing her silhouette and the bottle＊s while romantic orchestral music builds on the audio track. She approaches a house opposite and is let in by an older Latina woman with a larger build who catches the boys ogling her visitor. As she stares them down, one of the boys picks up a large, un-contoured, plastic bottle of Coca-Cola and holds it next to her sil-houette in his eyeline, with the ad .nishing with a caption that states ＆Inspira-cion Latina ＃ Latin Inspiration. Real.＊ The ad＊s clear expression of the association between the Coca-Cola bottle and alluring curvaceous femininity (as conceived by its target audience) is, if anything, ampli.ed by a gratuitous mockery of the older woman＊s .gure by its comparison to an un-contoured receptacle. 
The beach as locale and pretext 
In contemporary Western and, increasingly, global culture, the beach has both an objective reality (i.e. as an intertidal zone covered with loose particles such as sand, shingle, and/or pebbles) and a related cultural interpretation, percep-tion, and performance as a site associated with bodily pleasure, display, and relaxation. In the cooler latitudes, these associations are heavily stacked towards a relatively brief period of summer visitation. Conversely, the appeal of the tropics as a tourist destination is their notionally ＆endless summer＊and the consequent opportunity of engaging in beachside pursuits all year round. As Corbin (1994) has comprehensively argued, these perceptions and the socio-economic activities premised on them are distinctly modern phenomena. The subtitle of his in.uential book identi.es Western societies＊＆discovery＊of the seaside in the period 1750每1840 and his text also elaborates how this ＆dis-covery＊was a resigni.cation of certain coastal spaces for and by in.uential visitors to them. Key to this reconceptualisation and rebranding was the notion the coast, sea (or, at least, its immediate coastal shallows), and terrestrial beach areas constituted an invigorating environment that was eminently suitable for healthy physical pursuits. As beaches became visited and valorised by artists, writers, tourists, and entrepreneurs, particular coastal locations became para-digmatic sites for healthy leisure and provided models for other sites and for visitor perceptions of such sites. 
The earliest European beachside resorts developed at British resorts such as Scarborough and Brighton around the 1740s, with similar sites developing across the United Kingdom and Western Europe in subsequent decades. The United States was slow in adopting the trend but by the 1840s areas such as Coney Island had developed as key recreation areas for the residents of Brooklyn and Manhattan, with transport services moving increasing volumes of tourists to its shores as the nineteenth century progressed. Similarly, Atlantic City began to service the adjacent population of New Jersey in the 1850s and Revere Beach developed as a major destination for Bostonians shortly after. In the late nineteenth century the rapid expansion of Californian cities prompted the development of coastal resorts such as San Francisco＊s Ocean Beach. A new facet of beach tourism followed in Hawai＊i and California in the 1930s as sur.ng became popular, leading to locations such as Honolulu and Malibu becoming magnets for younger tourists. 
These developments were not without their controversies, particularly regarding the related exhibition of the female form. As Corbin (1994) identi-.ed, from the 1790s on men recognised the opportunities to view female bodies in contour-hugging garments occasioned by seaside bathing. Such opportunities persisted through the 1800s as women bathed in the sea, lakes, and pools in increasing numbers. ＆Moral panics＊ (Cohen, 1972) around their costumes and the ＆indecency＊ some observers and authorities ascribed to them increased in the early 1900s as some women began to adopt the sheer and abbreviated one-piece swimsuits pioneered by high-pro.le aquatic performers such as Annette Kellerman. Indeed, the latter was arrested by police on the grounds of indecent exposure while wearing such a costume at Boston＊s Revere Beach in 1908 (Hayward & Milner, 2017, p. 210). Moral anxieties of this kind resulted in police action against female bathers wearing one-piece out.ts at popular beachside locations in the 1910s每1920s, including arrests and the issuing of .nes. But such measures were increasingly ine.ectual as fashions and their acceptability outpaced self-appointed moral guardians and an o.cial ban on ＆swimming suits of the Annette Kellerman type＊ that was declared on Coney Island in 1930 (Unattributed 1930, p. 28) appears to have been more observed in its breach than by conformity to its restrictions. 
As a product developed and initially marketed in the United States in the 1880s每1890s, while national beach culture was in a major expansion phase, Coca-Cola had an opportunity to gain traction with coastal tourists. As dis-cussed above, in the late nineteenth and early twentieth century beach-side vendors sold Coca-Cola and similar drinks chilled on ice, making the con-sumption of such products by swim-suited holidaymakers a feature of summer tourism, and the advent of Coca-Cola＊s company-made vending machines o.ering cooled bottles for sale from 1929 onwards increased brand penetration across the nation, including along its East and West Coast beaches. 
Bodies, beaches, marketing 
Representations of the female face and .gure have featured prominently in Coca-Cola marketing since the early 1890s (see Beyer, 2000, p. 14). Since the introduction of calendars featuring ＆Coca-Cola Girls＊ in 1900 (ibid., 19), the association between young women＊s lightly clad curvaceous bodies and beaches .rst occurred in the 1920s (ibid., 83) and became prominent in the 1930s. In 1931 Coca Cola employed the young artist Haddon Sundblom to paint images of Santa Claus as a believably hearty (and rotund) .gure qua.ng Coca-Cola in a series of billboard and press advertisements, which established an enduring link between the brand and the Christmas season. In 1936 the company employed Sundblom to produce more summery images, most notably a series featuring young showgirl, Alice Anderson. Anderson .rst appeared in New York as one of the Roxyettes (later known as Rockettes) at the city＊s Roxy Theatre. Like her fellow performers, she was tall, slim, and agile. Sundblom painted her in several poses that highlighted her svelte .gure, long legs, attractive face, and wavy hair. His images were closely similar to the (now) ＆classic＊ pin-up art of the 1930s, such as those images produced by fellow Coca-Cola artist Gil Elvgren, who glamorised the faces and bodies of young Caucasian models by using soft, peachy skin tones, emphasising bodily curvature and appealing, smiling faces. The new age of Coca-Cola marketing symbolised by the company＊s use of such imagery was explicitly acknowledged in posters that commemorated the .rst 50 years of Coca-Cola, which appeared with variations of the header, ＆Thru 50 years ＃ 1886 to 1936＊. The artwork for one of these ads featured two young women reclining on abeach.3 The rear .gure is wearing blue stockings, a loose, blue one-piece bathing suit that ends below her knees, and has a collared top with her hair tucked into a blue bonnet. A second female is posed in front of the older .gure, symbolising modernity. Her legs are bare, and her yellow, scoop-necked bathing suit ends at her upper thighs. Her wavy, auburn hair is unrestrained, as if blowing in the breeze. The text below the image declares, ＆Fashions in clothes change. But human thirstisalwaysthe same.＊ Whatever the truth of this statement, it is also somewhat disingenuous, as what has principally changed over the period is the overtness of representations of female bodies in the public arena. 
One of Sunblom＊s best-known images of Anderson appeared two years later in a promotional poster headed, ＆Thirst asks nothing more＊ (see Agrippina, 2015). The image of Anderson in this work is similar to the previously dis-cussed ad in terms of bodily exposure, but is all the more e.ective for the artist＊s representation of his model with a dazzling smile and palpable sense of joy. The accompanying copy conveys Coca-Cola consumption as a highly sensual exercise: 
Make your place in the sun a cool one. Just tip a frosty bottle of Coca-Cola to your lips. Coca-Cola, ice-cold, is cooling ＃ so cooling. You thrill to its taste and the happy after-sense of refreshment that follows. 
In the ad, the beach is rendered simply, o.ering the barest pretext for the model＊s representation in swimming garb. The sand is a uniform, bleached cream colour while the water beyond is a deep indigo detailed only by one thin line of surf approaching the shore. Similar images followed in the 1940s in widely disseminated campaigns and the sexual suggestiveness of these images was made even more explicit in a well-known poster that debuted in 1946 (see Mooney, 2008). This featured Anderson (as a brunette), represented sideways on, wearing a white halterneck bikini top with plunging neckline on a thin line of sand with no visible background. She looks o. frame to the left with a delighted smile and inviting eyes at (what might be interpreted to be) a male .gure located outside the image, whose hand, holding a bottle of Coca-Cola, protrudes into the frame. Aside from a circular Coca-Cola logo, the image only features one word, ＆Yes＊, leaving the viewer to interpret what is being agreed to. In this context, the Coca-Cola bottle (like other, similar bottles) has a degree of polyvalence; while its contours may suggest female curves, its shape and solidity allow it to be interpreted as a phallic symbol in particular visual scenarios and/or when juxtaposed with, or titled towards, parts of the human anatomy.4 In this sense, the bottle in the ＆Yes＊ ad has a ripe duality. The woman depicted may have a ＆Coca Cola body＊ (in terms of the previously discussed vernacular uses of the term) but the bottle here is also readily com-prehensible as symbolising male desire (and the ＆Yes＊ as a statement of acquiescence to such desire). 
Having established such associations as a standard aspect of product market-ing, the imagery was taken further in a range of scenarios that showed ener-getic, (mostly Caucasian) young men and women engaged in beachside leisure 每 most notably in a series of television ads that appeared in the 1960s on North American, Western European, and Australasian television. The initial ads were interesting in their attempts to address and negotiate the .edgling youth market, which had emerged in the 1950s, and a wider demographic. One of the best examples of this is a one-minute-long TV ad, circa 1962, that encouraged viewers to buy six-packs of the drink.5 Opening with scenes of attractive young men and women dressed in beach attire sweating under a hot sun, having drunk all their Coca-Cola, the group＊s thirsts are relieved when a young man and woman appear bearing six-packs, swaying as they carry them in sync with the ad＊s soundtrack. Produced when the surf music style associated with bands such as Dick Dale and His Del-Tones and harmony ensembles such as Jan and Dean had not broken into the commercial mainstream, the ad＊s music is a curious mixture of calypso, South African kwela, and harmony vocals. Subsequent TV ads modernised a little further, featuring the jingle ＆Things go better with Coke＊, written by young advertising executive Bill Backer and recorded by pop-folk trio The Limeliters in 1963每1964. Attempt-ing to gain more credibility with a rapidly di.erentiated youth market 每 and noting the manner in which The Beach Boys had referenced the virtues of purchasing an ＆extra-large Coke＊ in their song ＆Drive-In＊ (on the band＊s 1964 album All Summer Long) 每 a number of fashionable performers were invited to blend Backer＊s jingle with their own music for a batch of tracks used in 1965. A prime example was Jan and Dean＊s version, which combined a surf-pop version of the jingle and a ＆drop-in＊ sequence of their recent hit single ＆Little Old Lady from Pasadena＊. 
By the 1970s Coca-Cola television ads produced for both the US and international markets often involved combinations of popular music (in various styles), youthful, minimally clad bodies, and beachside pursuits. An extensive series of such TV ads followed over the next two decades. In implicit recog-nition of the habitual objecti.cation of the barely clad female body in such material, a Dutch Coca-Cola ad circa 20046 shows a young woman in a skimpy red dress on a beach, drinking Coke Light and staring lasciviously at a hand-some young man emerging from the surf. Watching him slowly dry and dress (in what is presented as a ＆reverse striptease＊), she pulls suggestively at her neckline and lifts her skirt. As he turns, she is surprised to see that he has put on a priest＊s collar and shirt, only for him to approach her and touch her on the forehead, as if in blessing, and walk away. 
In the early 2000s Coca-Cola diversi.ed its brand development by sponsor-ing various socio-cultural activities designed to appeal to both broad and niche markets. One notable early example of this was its sponsorship 每 and related naming right 每 for the annual student spring break festivities held at South Padre Island, on Texas＊s Gulf Coast. The sponsoring involved creating a (tem-porary) ＆Coca-Cola Beach＊ adjacent to the Radisson Resort, with a live music stage and related activities that are ably summarised in the website copy for the 2013 event: 
It will no doubt be the biggest beach party of the year in Texas! The stage will be the hottest daytime party during spring break 2013. Coca-Cola beach will be the gathering place for tens of thousands of coeds to enjoy dance contests, bikini contests, live music, and DJ＊s [sic]. You＊ll meet groups such as the Dallas Cowboy Cheerleaders, the Miller Lite Girls, the Smokeless Tobacco Girls, and many more. Attempting to climb the Army tower is another favorite on the Isla Grand Beach. Kegs are allowed on the beach 每 but no glass bottles. Guests at the Isla Grand Beach Resort will have the upperhand on the largest spring break venue in the nation! If you want to stay where the party is, make reservations at the Isla Grand Beach Resort ＃ It＊s the biggest party in Texas ya＊ll! 
(South Padre, 2013, online) 
Association with the event involved a considerable degree of risk. As spring break activities are seldom associated with sobriety or sexual restraint, there was the potential to back.re on the brand＊s association with what might be termed the ＆good, clean＊ (if routinely sexualised) beachside fun represented in the brand＊s TV ads. Indeed, this became apparent when a series of violent incidents, including sexual assaults, prompted attendees to .le suits against Coca-Cola for inadequate security (see, for instance, Merlan, 2012). Such incidents led Coca-Cola to withdraw its sponsorship, with Rockstar Energy Drink taking over in 2016. In terms of branding strategies, the initial impetus can be seen to associate spring break and spring breakers with the product through the brand＊s tacit acceptance of the Bacchanalian nature of the event. In this regard, it can be seen to have been successful. 
Dance jamming 每 untrammelled co-creativity 
Whatever the short-term success of Coca-Cola＊s temporary branding of an area of South Padre island＊s beachfront as a company beach in the early 2000s, the spilling-over of libidinal energies into sexual assaults clearly represented a pro-blem for the brand. The violence associated with its seasonal beach property can be perceived as a form of adverse ＆collaborative co-creation＊ of the brand image, reversing the more potentially positive aspects of the collaborative con-sumer-brand relationship conceptualised by Iglesias et al. (2013). Researchers such as Aaker (1992) and Keller (1992; 2016) have emphasised the crucial role that ＆brand equity＊ has for businesses, with Keller de.ning the latter as ＆the di.erential e.ect that brand knowledge has on customer response to brand marketing activity＊(2016, p. 3). But for all their acuity in this regard, one area they do not characterise or theorise is the independent use of brand names, imagery, and associations by cultural agents who are in possession of brand knowledge and draw on that to produce cultural artefacts that refer to the brand in ways that are variously congruent, incongruent, and/or subversive of corporate strategies promoting the brand. This topic has recently been addressed by Kristal et al. (2018), who refer to the aforementioned activities as ＆non-collaborative co-creation＊of brands/brand equity 每with the ＆non-collaborative＊aspect referring to engagements and manipulations of the brand independent of company con-trol, oversight, or in.uence. The authors approach their topic with regard to two distinct approaches, which they term ＆brand play＊ and ＆brand attack＊.In the former, ＆the brand may serve as a fodder for parody and pranks＊(ibid., 335) that can be interpreted as ＆an indication of much-coveted cultural resonance for ori-ginal brand meanings＊(ibid., 337, Fournier & Avery, 2011). In the latter, ＆brand attack＊ represents a deliberate ＆d谷tournement＊ (DeBord & Wolman, 1956) or ＆culture jamming＊ (Klein, 1999) in which brands are subjected to subversive manipulation. In addition to using these two polarities to structure their argu-ment, Kristal and colleagues also identify two speci.c cohorts as being involved in these activities 每＆professional artists＊and ＆consumer activists＊. 
While Kristal et al.＊s discussions advance the understanding of the phenom-ena they address, they elide two interlinked aspects that can contribute to a more nuanced and less binary understanding of unauthorised co-creativity and the manner it manifests itself. Throughout their article, the authors posit a distinction between the ＆professional＊artist referred to in the article＊s abstract, who they generally associate with ＆brand play＊, and another type, the ＆inde-pendent＊ artist (ibid., 337), generally associated with ＆brand attack＊ by the authors. In terms of de.ning the term ＆artist＊, the authors only o.er a brief characterisation of the artists＊＆superior experience and expertise in design pro-cesses＊, their identi.ability as ＆experts＊, and their ＆social distance from other consumer co-creators and mere observers＊(ibid., 338). In terms of the media used by such artists, the authors write with the assumption that the term ＆artist＊ means, and will be understood to refer to, visual artists working with static image media (with this informing the design of the research project central to their article in which students responded to product logos and related visual images). As a result of their speci.c focus on either ＆playful＊professional artists or ＆attacking＊independent artists/activists, a variety of phenomena fall between the discursive cracks, including types of ＆vernacular co-creativity＊ such as the adoption of product associations into vernacular linguistic and related cultural contexts that are subsequently available to be developed by fans in manners that may deviate from and destabilise aspects of brand construction (ibid., 335). Within the latter, non-collaborative co-creation and what might be termed ＆brand fandom＊ operate as an unruly and unpredictable force, examples of which are discussed in subsequent paragraphs. 
Two contrasting examples of ＆brand fan play＊ serve to illustrate its unpre-dictability, those occurring with regard to two British brands 每 Burberry and Clarks. In the late 1990s每early 2000s the distinctive Burberry check design, featuring a tartan pattern on a beige background, became popular and, subse-quently, widely associated with a youth/socio-economic/regional subculture known as ＆chavs＊, which was widely associated with coarseness and/or crimin-ality in the mainstream media. The latter tendency was so marked that some venues banned individuals whose clothes featured the tartan altogether (Bothwell, 2005). Such associations were the antithesis of the brand the long-established Burberry company had sought to foster and the company responded in 2005 by reducing the number of products that used the distinctive check design (ibid.). This popular cultural appropriation and resigni.cation of a brand was disruptive to the brand and resulted in modi.cations to the company＊s product range and marketing strategies. A similar adoption by youth sub-cultures occurred with regard to footwear manufactured by the British com-pany Clarks over a more extended period. Unlike Burberry, Clarks has been a less prestigious producer, principally known for manufacturing sturdy children＊s school shoes since the 1940s. Along with the former, the company is known for its ankle-high, suede ＆desert boot＊ that was an unremarkable product until it was adopted by Jamaican ＆rude boys＊ (disenfranchised and criminalised youth who wore items of expensive clothing as a subcultural marker) in the 1960s, which contributed to their similar popularity with British mods in the same decade.7 While the company has never sought to exploit this aspect in its marketing, the association has acted as an independent driver of brand equity, particularly in Jamaica over a sustained period. The latter has been boosted by the frequent name-checking of the product in reggae and dancehall music tracks from the 1970s每present8 and through the product＊s visual representation in popular music videos for tracks such as Vybz Kartel and Popcaan＊s cele-bratory ＆Clarks＊ (2010) without any of the negative associations accruing to Burberry through association with chavs and without withdrawal of the product from the company range and marketing. 
While the Coca-Cola brand has not been appropriated and re-signi.ed by a youth subculture in the manner of Burberry or Clarks, it has been subject to a range of non-collaborative co-creations in the popular cultural sphere that draw on its vernacular symbolism. One notable, high-pro.le example of this drew on the previously discussed association of the product, the beach and (young, attractive) bodies through both linguistic terminologies and audio-visual representations. Illustrating the international di.usions of the afore-mentioned aspects, the single and accompanying music video for the ＆Cola Song＊ discussed below was co-written and sung by a Rumanian pop-house singer named Inna, co-written, produced, and arranged by Norwegian Andreas Schuller, American Thomas Joseph Rozdilsky, and Colombian J. Balvin (who also provided the reggaeton spoken passage in the track) and with the video being shot in Barcelona and Costa Rica by Spanish-American director John Perez. 
After gaining popularity in Europe with her lively pop-house tracks and gaining traction in the Mexican market with her 2013 album Party Never Ends, Inna signed to the US company Atlantic, which released ＆Cola Song＊ as a single in 2014. The .rst line of Inna＊s sung lyrics declare that, ＆We got that Coca-Cola bottle shape＊ and asks (the implicitly male) listeners whether they want to experience the singer＊s ＆sugar taste＊ before further suggestive lyrics and a Spanish language chorus that emphasises the singer/song/perception of femi-ninity communicated in the songs as ＆soy [＆so＊] Latina＊. The ambiguity as to the ＆we＊ in the lyrics is resolved in the accompanying music video, which shows Inna accompanied by two female back-up dancers. The video is shot on a tropical beach with the (young, slim, and stereotypically attractive) singer and back-up performers dancing on the sand wearing bikinis and swimsuits inter-spersed with shots of Inna swimming and cavorting in the sea and standing in the entrance to a cave. The dance movements and camera angles convey the singer and dancers as sexually ＆hot＊ in a manner common to reggaeton, Latinx pop, and house/dance music genres, unabashedly focusing on hips, posteriors, and breasts shaken enthusiastically. Underlining the association between the soft drink, Inna and female sexuality Balvin＊s rapped section 每 delivered in Colombian slang9 每 modi.es the previously discussed term ＆real Coca-Cola＊ by referring allusively to Inna as ＆pura＊ (i.e. pure) Coca-Cola from Romania who has come ＆here＊ (implicitly Latin America) to dance. 
As should be apparent from previous discussions, the song and video do not seek to establish novel connections and associations between female bodies, perceptions of desirably curvaceous femininity, and the distinctive shape of the Coca-Cola bottle, but rather provide the latest instantiation of an ongoing vernacular co-creation of brand associations .rmly established in vernacular language use. In this regard, the song and video might be regarded as a routine and inconsequential popular cultural text except for the degree of brand exposure it provided to a niche audience of young Latinx dance music fans, having secured an astonishing 205,607,905 views on YouTube to date.10 Given the highly sexualised nature of the song and video, it is unlikely that Coca-Cola would have produced such a product and, thereby, secured such wide exposure to a particular demographic. Given that there is no evidence that Coca-Cola sought to interfere with any aspect of the song and video＊s use of its product name, their strong brand promotion can be perceived to con.rm the strength and salience of Coke＊s previous marketing e.orts. If advertising can be understood as a process through which certain meanings are a.xed to brands, the song＊s and video＊s complementarity to previous marketing campaigns can be understood as a con.rmation and a.rmation of branding strategies. Indeed, Coca-Cola consolidated the success of Inna＊s product and her (unauthorised) prior association with the brand by featuring her in a one-minute-long Coke Zero video ad made for Rumanian TV in 2018.11 Inna is shown entering a hotel, listening to one of her dance tracks on headphones, and then going into a lift with a waiter delivering an ice-bucket of Coke Zero an upper .oor room. When the lift stalls between .oors, the star-struck hotel employee o.ers Inna a Coke, which she gratefully accepts and sips from. In return, she unplugs her headphones and the two then dance to the track brie.y in the lift while brandishing Coke bottles. As the door is forced open, the waiter takes a photo of himself and the singer smiling side by side. Emerging from the lift moments later, he checks his phone only to realise that the singer＊s image is obscured by the curvaceous Coca-Cola bottle she is holding in front of her. The ad is an artful recuperation of both the singer＊s independently made song and video and the linguistic .gure of the ＆Coca-Cola body shape＊ celebrated in its lyrics, manipulating these into a playful branding strategy as a reciprocal ＆co-creation＊. 
But while the Zero commercial may have reined in the overt sexuality of Inna＊s original music video, other related texts took the associations beyond those crafted by the singer and her creative team in the original products. While they appear to have only achieved a fraction of the viewing .gures of the original video, two pornographic collages based on the ＆Cola Song＊ appeared online in 2015 under the titles ＆Cola XXX＊ and ＆Inna 每 Cola Song Porn Music Video＊, juxtaposing sequences from the original music video with hardcore images of naked young women and of couples having sex on the beach. While their relatively low viewing .gures12 may not have represented a threat to the brand and its wholesome product image, they might be inter-preted as a particular form of pornographic ＆culture jamming＊ similar to that of the established ＆porn parody＊ genre that produces sexualised re-imaginations and revisions of well-known cinema texts. Indeed, Inna and Coca-Cola are far from alone in being re-interpreted in this manner, as there are a number of explicit porn videos available online whose titles refer to individuals involved in sexual activity while wearing the uniforms and on premises associated with companies such as McDonald＊s. One of the key di.erences with Coca-Cola products is that the latter parody/culture-jam brands whose marketing has not been strongly sexualised in any of the ways that Coca-Cola has, leaving it more open to such brand ＆attacks＊. 
Conclusion 
The socio-cultural engagements with the Coca-Cola brand detailed in this chapter can be understood as exercises in vernacular co-creation. Fortunately for Coca-Cola, these engagements have, in the main, been largely congruent with the brand image fostered by the company over an extended period and, indeed, have served to promulgate it in a variety of contexts. The unauthorised texts generated by vernacular co-creativity may not have been the ＆real thing＊ in terms of being o.cial promotional material, but they have certainly ＆added life＊ to the company＊s brand in manners that might have been considered too risqu谷 (and/or otherwise problematic) for its marketers. They also have sig-ni.cantly di.erent in.ections from either the parodic/pranking ＆brand play＊ or subversive ＆brand attack＊ approaches identi.ed by Kristal et al. (2018) and, instead, can be better understood to embody aspects of the ＆blank parody＊ attributed to pastiche within postmodernist discourse. Material produced in the 

latter mode deploys .gures, motifs and images from previous cultural texts, genres, and/or brands to create new articulations of these without ulterior and/ or ideological purposes. In some senses, the incidents and processes analysed in this chapter suggest the Coca-Cola brand has become an entity that extends and reproduces itself autonomously in the popular cultural sphere. In these respects, the brand has achieved an enviable self-su.ciency, despite in.ections that may jar with more conservative notions of propriety.  











13 Coke and the kids 
Viviane Riegel and Joana Pellerano 
Introduction 
The youth market has been integral to Coca-Cola 每 as a brand and as a pro-duct. This chapter explores this relationship by examining how young people interact with the brand, and how their own voices and ideas have been inte-grated into Coca-Cola＊s brand communication strategies. The signi.cance of this relationship has increased in recent years, with social media providing the platforms for this market to become louder and, indeed, more demanding. 
The Coca-Cola brand exempli.es what Kellner (2003) labels as media spec-tacle, where meaning is constructed through the spectacles a brand produces as a diverse experiential set of interactions present in consumers＊ everyday lives (Riegel, 2013). Competition between brands requires companies to make their logos as familiar as possible. They consequently adorn their products with them and insert them into advertisements, as well as other media spectacles like important sporting events, TV shows, and movies. However, these established approaches have not necessarily resonated with the lucrative youth market, which has developed new ways of experiencing brands and their communica-tion strategies. Brands hoping to engage this elusive youth market must increasingly work to establish themselves as a part of interactions within digital social media. Coca-Cola, which is representative of the way consumer culture .nds global success by linking itself to global ideals, has played an active role in creating and developing taste communities and sharing symbols, images, and experiences. The Coke brand forms a memory dimension, combining experi-ences of media consumption with the everyday personal lives of consumers across di.erent parts of the world. To this end, Coca-Cola aims to build global associations with the ideal of completeness, of possible happiness through the consumption of goods. 
A nuanced understanding of contemporary youth culture cannot be devel-oped without reference to global consumer culture. Globalisation has been signi.cantly shaped through the development of physical and virtual mobility, two factors which have, in turn, strongly a.ected younger generations. Their experiences of increased mobility and exposure to digital media are also informed by their embrace of multicultural perspectives, code switching, and the consumption of global trends. As a symbol of the globalisation of consumer culture, then, the brand has an identity that transcends ideological, territorial, and cultural borders. Brands therefore o.er something to individuals who understand and crave their representation around the world, a situation that Semprini (2006, p. 83) labels the ＆logic of the brand＊. 
This chapter explores the challenges faced by brands when dealing with the youth market, speci.cally within the competitive global sphere where Coca-Cola aims to maintain its dominance. Our analysis focuses on the experiences of young people with Coca-Cola, both as consumers and as producers of meaning for the brand. Experiences are analysed as ways to build symbolic universes, as well as spectacles of social practices, especially consumption (Moor, 2003). By analysing Coca-Cola＊s communication strategies in relation to the youth market, we can develop a more nuanced understanding of the consumption practices that result from the symbolic hegemony proposed by the brand, as well as the new meanings created in dialogue with the consumers＊ own ideas and demands. To this end, we contend that such practices occur in local settings, in spaces of communication and consumption within global cities, mainly through mediascapes that drive interactions with the brand (Riegel, 2013). 
An analysis of data collected in S.o Paulo between 2015 and 2016 regarding the interaction of young people (n=40) with global brands (Riegel, 2018) indicates that such relationships are mainly linked to the development of media technologies and new forms of communication, and experiences of consump-tion are concentrated around the diversity and multiplicity of global symbols. Where global food and beverage brands are present, they serve to in.uence individuals＊ eating and symbolic practices. Such practices are also related to the spatiality of cities 每 Coca-Cola advertising across multiple sites was frequently mentioned by respondents 每 and to interactions with the brand through sporting events. These interactions also occur through virtual media, in the form of games, videos, and posts (notably through digital social networks). Respondents noted the interactions generated by the presence of the Coke brand in key tourism sites and in other areas of high visibility (although many also made reference to obesity). One of the young individuals mentioned: ＆I drink Coca-Cola everywhere, in any moment, I even consider myself as addicted to it. The brand is present in my life, as a childhood memory, and generally as a friend＊ (Casaqui & Riegel, 2016, p. 237). This close connection to the global brand transforms it into an ordinary and familiar element in the respondent＊s daily consumption practices. 
Building on the .ndings from Riegel (2018), the empirical material devel-oped for this chapter consists of two parts. Firstly, it draws on the .ndings from three focus groups comprised of six individuals each, which discussed symbols and brand meanings. Secondly, it draws on 15 focus groups comprised of .ve individuals each, which examined the understandings and meanings associated with the brand＊s advertisement. All the individuals live in S.o Paulo and are aged from 14 to 16 years old. This .eld work was conducted between February and March 2019. The content of the discussions was based on the interactions with the Coca-Cola brand, concerning its symbols, the individuals＊ experiences with the brand in their everyday lives, and their memories and understanding of Coca-Cola＊sdi.erent messages as delivered by its advertisements. 
DNA of Coca-Cola 
A brand＊s DNA is formed through a combination of memories from media products and the personal experiences of each consumer. When discussing Coca-Cola＊s DNA, our .rst group of respondents were focused on the brand＊s symbols and their associations. 
The main symbols mentioned were the red colour and the brand＊s logo, as general representations; the polar bear and Santa Claus, as imaginaries the brand addresses; soda and taste, as sensorial experiences; and family and satisfaction, as emotional experiences. The general symbols are mainly associated with the presence of the brand in a wide variety of places, physical and digital, local and global. Respondents a.rmed that they attribute a feeling of omnipresence to Coca-Cola＊s logo, particularly in social media advertising. Many, however, viewed this negatively. The brand＊s presence was more strongly and positively related to physical experiences, such as drinking a Coke, and holding the can or the bottle. They mentioned the emotional ideas of happiness and of private memories less frequently and not as the most important connections with the brand. Some respondents even mentioned that they felt the communication e.ort of connecting positive emotions to Coca-Cola was in many ways arti.-cial, since it didn＊t correspond to their own sensations and feelings when they thought of the brand, or consumed one of its products. 
As Moor (2003) notes, consumption is but a single contact point (amongst many) with a brand. Other such contact experiences include physical urban spaces, as well as the virtual spaces created through digital networks. The per-ceived presence of Coca-Cola＊s symbols and its connection to spaces through di.erent experiences show how these young individuals consume, translate, and understand the brand＊s communication strategies. The symbols＊ omnipre-sence (previously mentioned as one of the main associations) is divided between the private and public spheres, each with di.erent perceptions. In the private space of the home it is related both to the everyday consumption of Coca-Cola＊s products, and to memories and recent events with family. The former is more connected to the physical and banal consumption of the brand. In turn, the latter is responsible for some of the emotional translations the respondents make of the brand, like mentioning birthday parties, Christmas, and other family celebrations. Within public spaces, stores and city streets are also understood as places for banal interaction with the brand. Yet, these con-nections are not the same in digital public spaces, such as social media (like Instagram and Facebook) or entertainment platforms (like YouTube and Spotify), where respondents reveal a negative reaction to the brand＊s exag-gerated presence. Many viewed Coke as something of an intruder, as it does not add anything to the entertaining experience they are sharing online with their friends and contacts. To this end, our respondents＊ concerns about the brand＊s over-the-top omnipresence reiterated what has already been found in previous studies of consumption of global brands by this age group (Riegel, 2018). The diversity of Coca-Cola＊s symbols did little to improve its standing, with many respondents reporting concerns about the brand＊s over-exposure, which created a perception of arti.ciality and distance. 
Considering the perspective of Brazilians as consumers of the global brand, respondents mentioned the connection of Coca-Cola to the idea of globalisa-tion and its worldwide presence. They also connected the brand to travel experiences and to international symbols. However, they also linked Coke with its country of origin, the United States. To this end, they expressed a strong connection between Coke and American society in relation to consumerism, fast food, and obesity. However, these young Brazilians also saw the brand as part of their own local context. This not only re.ected their own immediate proximity to Coke as a product and as a brand, but also the many individual and private experiences involving Coke they have in their everyday lives. 
Other perspectives from the margins of globalisation point to the limitations of a simple binary between the local (authentic; diverse; free) and the global (arti.cial; standardised; imposed). In contemporary society, the homogenisation of consumption occurs through the process of globalisation. The growth dynamic of globalisation, according to George Ritzer and Mike Ryan (2004), follows three processes: capitalism, Americanisation and McDonaldisation. This dynamic explains the di.erent perceptions held by our respondents, which simultaneously positioned Coke as a product of bland global capitalism and as a symbolic artefact that was informed by deeply personal experiences. 
Di.erent meanings and understandings 
Our second group of respondents explored the meanings and understandings attributed to Coca-Cola through discussions of their memories and interactions with Coke advertising. The memories associated with Coca-Cola＊s commu-nication campaigns emphasised: emotions (through the idea of ＆taste the feeling＊ and happiness), Christmas (polar bear and Santa Claus), celebrity (both of local YouTubers and artists, and of themselves, with bottles and cans showing their names), energy and health (sugar-free products and physical activities), and sports events and sponsorship (the FIFA World Cup and the Olympics). 
In order to explore these meanings in greater detail, the respondents were shown recent Coca-Cola advertising campaigns and marketing activities in the Brazilian market (some of which were speci.cally named by the young inter-viewees). These campaigns and themes included: Taste the Feeling; Christmas; Health/Energy; Names and Artists; and World Cup and Olympics. 
The ＆Taste the Feeling＊ campaign was broadcast in Brazil in 2016. Two videos from the campaign were shown to our respondents.1 One respondent succinctly summarised the two campaigns: the .rst video shows ＆Avicii and a guy that is probably Conrad Sewell recording the song in a studio; [＃] various people drinking a bottle of Coca-Cola and getting happy, smiling. [＃] The second video is a cover [of the same song] by [Brazilian singer] Luan Santana.＊ In terms of the symbols featured in this campaign, the respondents noted bot-tles, cans, trucks, and billboards with Coke＊s brand and lettering, the colour red, and the campaign slogan. They associated this commercial and, more generally, the brand with a sense of joy, energy, togetherness, and ＆good feelings in general＊that are experienced by building ＆a history together with Coca-Cola＊. Our respondents felt that this campaign conveyed a global message that was not related to any speci.c culture, even though one of the advertise-ments was produced locally. In their opinion, the brand wanted to separate itself from other beverage brands by using a catchy song and emotional moments of connection. The e.ectiveness of such appeals was queried by some respondents: ＆In my two cents, the ad is too dramatic and not realistic because your problems aren＊t going to be solved by a bottle of Coca-Cola＊. 
The Christmas theme was discussed by our respondents in reference to two advertisements from 2016.2 One respondent o.ered the following outline of the two advertisements: ＆The .rst one is more connected with cold countries where it normally snows in the winter, [the] season of Christmas in the USA＊, and ＆shows a kid that wants to share happiness with others, so he delivers a Coca-Cola bottle＊; the second ad ＆shows us poor people in Brazil＊who felt joy by getting to meet Santa Claus for the .rst time. Respondents identi.ed the campaign＊s use of key symbols, including the Coke logo, the distinctive curved bottle, and the colour red (as featured on Coke materials and, indeed, Santa＊s out.t). Drinking Coca-Cola was associated with happiness and ＆having a good time and good memories＊. Respondents again identi.ed the commercials＊ messages within a global setting. One described them as being ＆for everyone and everywhere＊, while another explained that ＆[s]ince Coca-Cola is an international company, it sends the message to the whole world, and di.erent cultures make some adaptations to make it more viable for their culture＊.
