They did, however, identify some paradoxes. ＆The ads use Christmas as a method of transmitting a good message of great moments＊, explained one respondent, before observing, ＆Coke is an unhealthy drink and [we are] not sure that it will bring happiness to us＊. 
The connections between Coca-Cola and celebrity culture were explored by examining two separate campaigns: the 2014 ＆Share a Coke＊campaign,3 which ＆makes use of names of normal people＊on cans and bottles; and a 2017 Brazi-lian campaign,4 which encouraged music fans to buy cans and bottles featuring their favourite artists ＆and to make a competition among artists, so it＊s like a game＊. Our respondents believed that these campaigns sought to associate both ＆normal＊and ＆famous＊people with the brand＊s logo, bottles, and cans with a view to increasing consumption. The campaigns＊＆language, artists, and names＊ meant that messages taken from the campaigns displayed a stronger awareness of the campaigns＊localness. Respondents viewed the local/global dichotomy of the campaigns in mixed ways. One respondent thus noted how the ＆Share a Coke＊ campaign was a ＆global message that promotes selling Coca-Cola with names so people would buy it＊, while another felt that the 2017 campaign o.ered ＆a good way to call Brazilians＊ attention to buy Coca-Cola＊. 
The uneasiness about the connection between soda and obesity came out strongly when our respondents were shown two advertisements about Health/ Energy. The .rst was a 2017 North American ad on health awareness.5 The second was a 2013 Brazilian ad called ＆Positive Energy＊,6 which explained that the beverage contained 123 calories ＆of positive energy＊ to be used in fun activities such as walking the dog, laughing, or hugging. Respondents again noted the brand＊s logo, colours, and bottle. They also identi.ed a message concerning the possibility of choice and health o.ered by some of the brand＊s products. This message was understood both as global (＆This ad is global, because Coca-Cola is a global business that acts worldwide＊) and local (＆[It＊sa] local message, for the ones that drink Coke＊). Respondents also identi.ed a very di.erent message. Noting that ＆[n]owadays society is thinking more on health＊, one respondent noted how Coke was trying to o.er healthier choices and that it was in fact telling them to drink less soda, as it ＆will get [you] a lot of calories for the rest of the day＊. 
Sport is an important part of both Brazilian culture and youth culture. Respondents noticed Coca-Cola＊se.orts to connect itself with large sporting events, such as the World Cup (shown in a British ad for the 2018 tourna-ment7) and the Olympics (displayed in an ad for the torch relay for 2016＊s games in Brazil8). One respondent noted that ＆Coca-Cola associates sports, happiness, and the global importance of the brand with this ad＊. In reinforcing the connection between the brand, sports, and health, the campaigns also gave the impression that ＆Coca-Cola is always in the special moments of your life＊. Respondents additionally identi.ed a global message, ＆because it represents the cultures worldwide in events that everybody has knowledge of＊, even though ＆[i]t＊s more focused and connected to Brazil, due to the fact that the Olympics took place in Brazil, revealing some cultural aspects in a way to connect to the local people＊. While they viewed the 2016 advertisement as a reminder of the sporting event＊s spirit of ＆celebration, compassion and competitiveness＊, they also believed that the brand could bring even more joy to the mix. Some respondents observed, somewhat prosaically, that ＆[t]he purpose of the ad is to spread the message of the World Cup and the Olympics, through the Coca-Cola brand＊, while others were motivated to think more broadly about the campaign＊s themes and its signi.cance: ＆It＊s a commercial that we as a group think is very beautiful but we also think that the cultures should be together and united all the times and not just on these special dates and events that we eventually have＊. 
Conclusions 
In analysing the young Brazilians＊ understanding of various Coca-Cola advertising campaigns and their themes, we can .nd similarities and di.erences regarding the brand＊s symbols, associations, and global messages. Of the symbols featured in the campaigns, respondents readily recognised the presence of the product (along with bottles and cans), as well as the Coke logo and the colour red. Some speci.c symbols, such as Santa Claus and global sporting events like the Olympics, were viewed as ＆imaginaries＊ which sought to create a fantasy; a di.erent image for the brand than the one connected to its products. Although the young Brazilians displayed a recognition and awareness of the brand＊s more positive associations, they also showed a critical perspective regarding any asso-ciations that tried to distance the brand from the e.ects of drinking its products. 
During their discussions of Coca-Cola＊s advertising campaigns our respon-dents identi.ed a number of associations linked with positive feelings, like joy, energy, togetherness, happiness, and fun. This illustrates the degree to which the symbolic consumption proposed by Coca-Cola＊s communication strategies has focused on emotions and feeling (mainly the idea of happiness) as well as the degree to which this audience recognises and understands these emotional cues. It also illustrates the pervasiveness and coherence of Coke＊s marketing strategies. Coca-Cola＊s ＆Happiness＊ campaign commenced in 2006 with the .lm ＆Happiness Factory＊ (analysed by Carrascoza et al., 2008), about the pro-duction process of happiness. In the following instalment of the campaign from 2011, so-called ＆Happiness Machines＊ saw vending machines ＆come to life＊ (Casaqui, 2012) to emerge as objects of a.ection in their own right (Casaqui & Riegel, 2016). The experiences of happiness proposed by Coca-Cola were also analysed by Riegel and Gama (2014), through the strategies used by the brand to objectify the feeling of being happy anywhere in the world, based on di.erent representations of the common good. 
Other associations, like celebrities, health, and sports, were evaluated di.erently. The campaign engaging in celebrity culture was viewed in parti-cularly positive terms by our young Brazilian respondents. But in the case of health and sports, the images and ideas were seen as more problematic. Respondents noted that these associations seemed fake, and distant from the products that the brand sells. This was emphasised when some respondents mentioned the responsibility of the brand regarding obesity, especially among children and young people. 
Like their connection with happiness, Coca-Cola＊se.orts to connect itself with sport is part of an ongoing campaign. Its association with the Olympic Games is long-running, formally dating back to 1928. This connection is fur-ther enhanced by the close ties they have developed with the event＊s key symbols and practices; Coca-Cola bought the sponsorship rights for carrying the Olympic .ame for the 2016 Rio de Janeiro Olympics and, with it, the Olympic torch relay. The passage of the .ame through Brazil during the months leading up to the Games connected Coke with the athletes carrying the .ame, as well as audiences along the .ame＊s route to the Olympic city. Coke＊s sponsorship of the ＆Future Flames＊ campaign, which involved commu-nities nominating young people to carry the Olympic torch, similarly 







reinforced the brand＊s connection with the Olympics and, indeed, its key demographic. Many of the messages in the advertisements shown to our focus groups were regarded as being global, even in those cases where the advertisements were locally produced. This can in part be explained by the nature of the brand itself. As our respondents succinctly observed: ＆it is a global brand＊; ＆it＊sAmerican＊. However, our respondents were also cognisant of the brand＊se.orts to localise itself. Coke＊s e.orts to adapt its message to the local market was praised by our respondents. Signi.cantly, they interpreted these e.orts in a pragmatic way, noting that such adaptations are necessary for any global brand that wants to be successful. At a broader level, the ideas and emotions that our young Brazilian respon-dents identi.ed with Coca-Cola and its marketing o.er an understanding of the ways that global brands are produced and consumed in di.erent parts of the world. The respondents not only readily identi.ed and understood the brand＊s symbols and key associations; they were also aware of its global presence, and often displayed a desire for it. Yet, at the same time, they also called for the establishment of clearer boundaries and greater objectivity in the messages transmitted by the brand, with a view to eliminating or reducing fake and unrealistic images and impressions to consumers. The in.uence of global brands in contemporary society can be recognised as the result of strategic advertising, astute sponsorship of existing events, and the innovative expansion of the brand across multiple .elds and territories (includ-ing urban and virtual spaces, as well as packaging and product lines). Despite its status as a global brand, our respondents viewed Coke as both local and global and celebrated this duality. Signi.cantly, such outcomes are consistent with the .ndings from Riegel＊s (2018) analysis of global brands as symbols in global cities and as elements of local daily life. Our respondents＊ discussions reveal that the most signi.cant challenges that global marketers face in dealing with the youth market concern the much-needed limits regarding the presence of these brands in their everyday lives and practices, as well as the need for greater discretion in terms of messages that promulgate unrealistic images. These social and ethical perspectives are important contributions to the study of brands around the world. 

14 Coke and health 
Ella Chorazy 
In recent years, soft drinks (sodas) have lost their lustre. A progressive cultural push towards healthier consumption habits has taken its toll. Pressure on the food and beverage industry to address public health concerns intersects with health discourses aggressively advocating consumer responsibility for their own health behaviours. This chapter reviews public health concerns associated with Coca-Cola products, with a focus on diet soft drinks and the use of arti.cial sweeteners. Although sugar has a major part in health discourse, this issue and the medical evidence, as well as the societal impact, has been argued at con-siderable length elsewhere (see Appleton & Jacobs, 2011; Lustig, 2013; Nestle, 2015; 2018; Taubes, 2016). Those discussions examine the role of sugar swee-tened beverages, the industry, and consumers in the current obesity crisis and other preventable diseases. Instead, this chapter explores under-investigated aspects of Coca-Cola and its relationship with health through history, from its early days as a curative and curiously addictive tonic to its current o.erings of sugar-free varieties, which appeal to health-conscious consumers. The discus-sion is framed from a Western perspective, using three examples 每 TaB, Diet Coke, and Coke Zero 每 to contextualise Coca-Cola＊s response to public health concerns that have beleaguered the beverage industry, speci.cally rising rates of obesity and reported links between arti.cial sweeteners and cancer. These cases are considered in relation to modern scienti.c research, and the ethical tensions that have challenged Coca-Cola as it has attempted to counter these concerns and shift health discourse in di.erent directions. 
Curative Coke and medicinal marketing 
Early concerns about Coke＊s relationship were articulated through the question of whether or not the original Coca-Cola contained cocaine. Despite evidence to the contrary, the Company has long denied this (Hays, 2004, p. 102). Pendergrast (2013, pp. 51每4) con.rmed the original formula included cocaine in su.cient quantities to have an e.ect on consumers, but that the actual amount is debated. Nestle similarly substantiated that ＆coca leaves, which con-tain cocaine, were basic ingredients in the original Coca-Cola, but the Com-pany now processes out all traces of the drug and has done so for more than a century＊ (2015, p. 17). While the inclusion of cocaine may have contributed to Coca-Cola＊s initial popularity and habitual consumption, restrictions on the use and sale of cocaine were not legislated by the US Congress until 1914, so its presence in Coke was not illegal. Coca-Cola＊s removal of cocaine in 1903 may have been socially motivated rather than health related. According to Hamblin (2013) and Hale (2013), Southern newspapers were propagating racist perceptions of supposed African-American drug use and violence amidst the surging popularity of cocaine as an addictive, recreational narco-tic. Either way, Pendergrast (2013, p. 54) argues the cocaine controversy attached a sinful stigma to the brand that hardly helped sales, and Coca-Cola has adamantly attempted to avoid it ever since. Yet Coke＊s association with health discourse runs much deeper than its questionable cocaine contents. Stemming from its well-known origins as a pharmaceutical invention in 1886, the original syrup was increasingly popularised on the basis of its curative properties. 
The rapid industrialisation of the late 1800s changed the speed and rhythm of everyday life. ＆Coca-Cola emerged from this turbulent, inventive, noisy, neurotic new America. It began as a ※nerve tonic§ like many others marketed to capitalize on the dislocations and worries of the day [＃] [and] foisted upon the public by hopeful marketers during the golden age of quackery＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, pp. 8每9). At this time, the medical industry was still dan-gerously inconsistent in its approaches, and treatment e.cacy was largely dependent on the competency of the individual apothecary or physician. It was not until the early 1900s that studies argued for the adoption of a model that ensured ＆scienti.c rigor and educational excellence＊ as part of medical training (Irby et al., 2010, p. 220). Hamowy (1979, p. 73) noted that the ＆the exigencies of the market alone determin[ed] who would prove successful in the .eld＊ and resulting medical approaches ＆came to be known as ※heroic therapy§ and certainly killed large numbers of patients unfortunate enough to undergo treatment at the hands of its practitioners＊. As irregular and unorthodox medicines abounded, patented nostrums often provided cheaper and safer alternatives for common ailments such as indigestion (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 9). 
Indeed, Coke was sold as an aid to digestion, in addition to its power as a stimulant (even after the removal of cocaine) that ＆revives and sustains＊, ＆relieves mental and physical exhaustion＊, and is above all ＆Delicious! Refreshing! Exhilarating! [and] Invigorating!＊ (Troy, 2014). Although Coca-Cola .rstentered thepatentmedicine marketwhen itwas already highly saturated, it bene.ted from the therapeutic legacy of carbonated water that drove the original popularisation of soda fountains and their institutionalisa-tion as a drugstore staple (Pendergrast, 2013, pp. 13每15). Early marketing di.erentiated Coke from regular .avoured soda fountain syrups by selling it as a medicinal tonic, though over time abandoned this position in favour of e.ective establishment as an evergreen product aligned with everyday life (Pendergrast, 2013, pp. 137每8). 
Diet drinks and health-conscious consumers 
Aside from cocaine, other ingredients in Coca-Cola products have caused public consternation, including sugar (see Nestle, 2015), ca.eine (see Carpenter, 2014), and arti.cial sweeteners. This chapter focuses on the latter, particularly saccharin and cyclamate. The safety of arti.cial sweeteners is contested territory within the scienti.c literature as well as public suspicion of anything new and ＆unnatural＊ (Warner, 2011). Further, there is little universal agreement over the safety of speci.c types. For example, cyclamate is approved for use in Canada but banned in the US, while saccharin is approved in the US but banned in Canada. As a result, diet products from di.erent regions may taste di.erentasa result of variations in the arti.cial sweeteners used. This not only leads to product varia-tion between countries 每 despite often identical branding 每 but contributes to consumer confusion and arguably undermines their purchasing con.dence. Nevertheless, these chemicals o.er sweet yet calorie free (i.e. non-nutritive) alternatives for health-conscious consumers. 
Sodium saccharin, the world＊s .rst arti.cial sweetener, was discovered by accident in 1879 by scientists investigating derivatives of coal tar as possible food preservatives (De la Pe.a, 2010a, p. 160; Bridge, 2019, p. 136). Produc-tion of saccharin was commercialised in the mid-1880s (Bridge, 2019, p. 136) and a wholesale strategy was adopted to market it to beverage manufacturers (including Coca-Cola) as a cheaper alternative to sugar that could be blended with base soda syrups to stretch its supply (De la Pe.a, 2010a, p. 160). Initially classi.ed as an adulterant under the US Pure Food and Drug Act, the use of saccharin was temporarily prohibited, prompting Coca-Cola to remove it from its formula (Warner, 2011, p. 185). However, the public declaration that saccharin was ＆injurious to health＊ was met with political opposition 每 then-President Theodore Roosevelt＊s doctor recommended the sweetener as a diet-ary supplement to combat Roosevelt＊s obesity每 and it was (re-)approved for use conditional on appropriate labelling (Taubes, 2016, pp. 136每7). 
However, saccharin as a sugar substitute lacked popular appeal and took some time to be readily accepted by consumers. For the .rst half of the twen-tieth century, ＆saccharin was widely regarded as an inferior substitute, a symbol of corporate greed, and a product suitable only for use by diabetics＊ (De la Pe.a, 2010a, p. 160). Wary of damaging its brand, Coca-Cola encouraged the idea that products using arti.cial sweeteners were cheap and nasty alternatives to the real thing. World War II restrictions changed public perceptions, as sugar became a maligned symbol of excess and unnecessary, indulgent consumption (Bridge, 2019, p. 136). This shift was accelerated by both nutrition science connecting sugar consumption with obesity and the burgeoning diet industry that grew o. the back of ＆the battle of the bulge＊ and America＊s desire to be slim, trim, and lean (Taubes, 2016, pp. 132每3). 
In 1952 Kirsch Beverages commenced laboratory exploration of arti.cial sweeteners as palatable dietary solutions for hospital patients su.ering from diabetes and cardiovascular illnesses (Saxon, 1986). Within the year, they had developed the world＊s .rst sugar-free soda: No-Cal. Sweetened with sodium cyclamate, No-Cal avoided the metallic aftertaste left by saccharin and other synthetic chemicals. Its release also came at a time when American consumers were increasingly health conscious. After the correlation between high fat diets, high cholesterol and heart disease was established in the late 1940s, the scien-ti.c community began to advocate for low-fat diets and weight loss (La Berge, 2008; De la Pe.a, 2010a; 2010b). This discourse intensi.ed over the second half of the twentieth century and became the dominant ideology which shaped a near-universal approach to health that was validated by research, supported by the food industry, and promoted by popular media, healthcare practitioners, and public institutions (La Berge, 2008; De la Pe.a, 2010a; 2010b). Sugar was also singled out for its link to health problems, with arti.cial sweeteners increasingly used from the 1950s onwards as non-caloric sugar substitutes in both liquid and solid diet products (Smith, 2009, pp. 249每50). As a product innovation, No-Cal was the .rst entirely arti.cially sweetened carbonated beverage and its arrival marked the start of an age where ＆diet＊ food and drinks were set to boom. 
Despite No-Cal＊s early success, it failed to capitalise on its market-leading position as other competitors entered the fold. The .rst widely available sugar-free soda in the US was Royal Crown (RC) Cola＊s Diet Rite (now owned by Keurig Dr Pepper). Originally launched as a speciality dietetic product in 1958, it entered the national market in 1962 following positive consumer testing. Diet Rite enjoyed a temporary monopoly with their product marketed as a lifestyle choice with the tagline ＆Feel all right＊. While this was similar to No-Cal＊s approach, it broadened the appeal of Diet Rite beyond the medicinal market by entreating consumer taste expectations. The success of this strategy was re.ected in its expanded distribution and availability in mainstream food stores. Although diet drinks only accounted for approximately 1.5 per cent of the market at the time, RC＊s pricing strategy 每 where Diet Rite was sold at the same price as RC＊s regular cola 每 kick-started a craze for low-calorie options (Klein.eld, 1982). Eager to have a share in the emerging market of calorie-conscious consumers, Coca-Cola introduced TaB Cola in 1963, only days apart from PepsiCo＊s trial of Patio (subsequently rebranded as Diet Pepsi in 1964). 
Diet drinks grew in popularity to command more than a tenth of US soft drink consumption by the late 1960s (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 263), which dou-bled to 20 per cent by the 1980s (Smith, 2009, pp. 249每50). Today, despite declining soft drink sales, diet varieties share approximately 30 per cent of the overall carbonated beverage market (Nestle, 2015, p. 22). Diet Coke 每 sold as Coca-Cola Light in some countries 每 is currently the most valuable diet soda brand and most widely consumed (Foster, 2020). Sales have even occasionally toppled regular (i.e. full sugar) Pepsi from its position as the world＊s second-best selling soft drink (Nestle, 2015, p. 25). Although Diet Rite is still produced, it never regained its dominance after RC Cola encountered a combination of ＆bad luck, bad judgement, and a fateful ingredient known as cyclamate＊ (Wells, 2016). Despite the booming success of diet drinks in the .fties and sixties, emerging scienti.c research linked cyclamate (and saccharin) to cancer threa-tened to collapse the market and has left lingering questions about the safety of arti.cial sweeteners. 
TaB and the great cyclamate debate 
As with saccharin, cyclamate was discovered by accident, and it was not the .rst 每 or the last 每 arti.cial sweetener to be described as hazardous (Packard, 1976, p. 332; Lewis, 2001, p. 4). Patented and commercialised from the start of the 1950s, cyclamate was the original sugar substitute in all the top brands＊ diet soft drinks, including Coca-Cola＊s TaB. Coke was reluctant to throw support behind a new product that could threaten its .agship o.ering. Yet the new market segment was quickly growing too big to ignore. When research found 28 per cent of Americans were paying attention to their weight, the Coca-Cola Company launched ＆Project Alpha＊ to formulate and design its own diet soda (Klein.eld, 1982). Despite rumours suggesting TaB was an acronym for ＆Totally Arti.cial Beverage＊, the new product was named following exhaustive e.orts by advertising executives and suggestions autogenerated by Coca-Cola＊s market research computer (Plasketes, 2004, p. 55). Thought to be an e.ective choice for marketing to consumers who wanted to monitor 每 or keep tabs on 每 their weight, TaB was launched with the slogan ＆how can one calorie taste so good＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 263). 
Like RC＊s Diet Rite, Coca-Cola sold TaB with lifestyle messages that balanced taste and pleasure with beauty and health. However, TaB＊s separate identity re.ected Coca-Cola＊s concern that a diet drink would dilute their own brand and risk injuring traditional soda sales through a combination of self-cannibalization and exacerbation of public health concerns around sugar and diet. In contrast PepsiCo had long seen the advantage of including low calorie o.erings as part of the brand＊s main product line 每 both pragmatically in terms of reduced advertising cost as well as consumer perception bene.ts from extending their brand equity 每 with the relative popularity of Diet Pepsi a testament to the strength of this strategy. Pendergrast (2013, p. 263) suggested the Coca-Cola＊s ambivalent approach to producing a diet cola relegated it to initially holding a much smaller market position, with TaB＊s market share in the 1960s substantially less than Diet Pepsi or RC＊s Diet Rite. 
Scienti.c studies .rst indicated the potential carcinogenic properties of cyclamate in the late 1960s when laboratory rodents fed high dosages devel-oped malignant bladder tumours (see Price et al., 1970). Coca-Cola and its competitors were initially dismissive of the research, with one Coke executive suggesting ＆You＊d drown before you＊d get cancer＊ (Dickson, cited in Pendergrast, 2013, p. 275). However, ongoing negative .ndings, coupled with sensationalised media coverage and public panic, led Coca-Cola to pre-emptively recall its cyclamate-sweetened products (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 275). When research also connected cyclamate with embryotic birth defects (see Becker, 1970), the market for diet soft drinks nosedived (Plasketes, 2004, pp. 56每7). Research continued to link both cyclamate and saccharin with health issues through-out the 1960s and 1970s, although it was cyclamate that received the most public vili.cation and regulatory restrictions (De la Pe.a, 2010b; Warner, 2011). By 1970, it ceased to be a safe chemical and was banned in the US and elsewhere. Meanwhile, saccharin was approved for use in the US albeit with the mandated use of warning labels. Coca-Cola rapidly reformulated TaB to replace cyclamate with saccharin. Consumers surprisingly took to TaB＊s new, noticeably metallic .avour. E.ective marketing in the aftermath of the cyclamate scandal saw it shift from diet cola underdog to the leader of the pack, while the early innovators such as No-Cal and Diet Rite faded into obscurity (Seigel, 2006, p. 4). 
Almost two decades passed before Coca-Cola was prepared to attach their iconic red and white logo and trade dress to a diet product. Diet Coke was the result of Coca-Cola＊s ＆Project Harvard＊, which was engaged in future forecast-ing and banked on diet drinks as a growing market despite ongoing concerns about the safety of arti.cial sweeteners (Klein.eld, 1982). At the time, TaB had eclipsed its competitors and was the industry＊s top-selling diet soda, com-manding 4 per cent of all soft drink sales with a ＆rabidly loyal＊ fan base (Belkin, 1984). Such success emboldened Coca-Cola to entrust the Coke name to a new product, which it hoped would re-energise the Company＊s then-stagnat-ing image (Pendergrast, 2013, pp. 315每16). Diet Coke＊s introduction in 1982 ultimately signalled TaB＊s progressive decline, though its cultural status has been immortalised by popular culture appearances and small yet persistent consumer demand has ensured the iconic pink-canned drink is still produced (Holson, 2018). 
The dominance and decline of Diet Coke 
Diet Coke was ＆the most carefully developed and researched product＊ in Coca-Cola＊s history (Dyson, cited in Pendergrast, 2013, p. 323). Rather than taking a cosmetic approach to its advertising, the new beverage was introduced with the tagline ＆just for the taste of it＊, and the .rst commercials ＆had a heavy dose of men＊, including then-celebrities Judd Hirsch and Phil Esposito (Klein.eld, 1982). This aimed to emphasise the diet product＊s broader appeal in terms of both .avour and consumer identity, and was in line with Coke＊s established brand equity. The strategic planning and investment paid o. as it quickly became a global phenomenon. Pendergrast (2013, p. 323) notes that Diet Coke＊s initial positioning as a gender-neutral lifestyle drink deliberately targeted yuppies and the emerging entrepreneurial class that was appropriate for the aerobic-crazed, appearance-driven ethos of the 1980s. This niche pro.ling was consistent with advances in market research and advertising practice, which increasingly relied on sophisticated techniques to understand and reach con-sumers. It positioned the drink as a classy choice preferred by the powerful in a hedonistic and ambitious age where personal and professional boundaries were being blurred. ＆Diet Coke had become a part of that powerful braid＊ where there was a convergence of ＆celebrity culture, commercial branding, and creativity＊ (Heller, 2018). By the mid-1990s, however, sales stalled, and its advertising budget was slashed after a series of campaign mis.res (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 375). Although Diet Coke still ranked among the most popular soft drinks, in less than a decade, it had gone from Coca-Cola＊s smash hit to ＆problem child＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 380). 
Diet Coke generated plenty of media coverage with novel advertising 每 such as the memorable ＆Diet Coke Break＊ commercials or the 2011 Karl Lagerfeld campaign 每 but this failed to translate into sales. Male consumers dwindled as the brand＊s identity narrowed and it assumed increasingly feminine connota-tions (Heller, 2018). Diet Coke abandoned all health discourses in favour of fashion. The product＊s style and sophistication were emphasised in an e.ort to target independent, aspirational women. Yet the diet soft drink market con-tinued to stagnate as people became less focused on calorie control and more interested in healthy nutrition. The overall market for carbonated soft drinks shrank as new products fragmented the marketplace and consumers turned to other purportedly healthier o.erings (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 418). Beverages that were perceived as more natural gained favour: waters (still or sparkling, with or without added vitamins, minerals, or .avours, etc.); juices (fruit-based, vege-table-based, cold-pressed, concentrates, smoothies, etc.); teas (iced, infused, fermented, etc.); and sports and energy drinks. While Coca-Cola was able to exploit these new segments, its arti.cially (i.e. chemically) sweetened ＆diet＊ branded mainstay struggled to make any new ground. 
Part of this strategy saw Diet Coke Plus (with added vitamins) introduced in 2007. This set the Company up for a number of lawsuits, as regulators like the Food and Drug Administration in the US declared the product mislabelled on the basis ＆that it wasn＊t legitimate to fortify junk foods＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, 
p.457). Although Coca-Cola persisted with the claim that its product couldbe enjoyed as part of a healthy, balanced lifestyle, the .edgling ＆functional＊ pro-duct was not popular. It was particularly hindered by its language; the word ＆diet＊was shamefully dated and the word ＆plus＊ was antithetically associated with beingplus-size. It was discontinued by 2011, though other variants have since beenintroduced with other additives including co.ee, green tea antioxidants, and even.bre. Coca-Cola Fiber+ has been sold in Japan since 2017 and was purported toboost digestive health, supress fat absorption, and moderate triglyceride levels inthe blood after eating (Bhattacharya, 2018). It is sugar-free and contains indiges-tible dextrin, a dietary .bre that commonly acts as a laxative. Harking back toCoke＊s original medicinal marketing 每 including symbolic red and white packa-ging 每 it was developed to cater to a ＆fast-growing segment where ingredients areadded to beverages to address speci.c dietary needs＊ (Quincy, cited in Taylor,2017). While it was ridiculed internationally, it successfully appealed to a small,unique group of health-conscious consumers in Japan (Bhattacharya, 2018).
The cultural trend favouring natural and ＆whole food＊ ingredients also saw the popularisation of alternative sweeteners, such as stevia. Coca-Cola uses stevia extract (steviol glycosides) as a sweetener in several of its subsidiary 
products, including Energy Brands＊ (Glac谷au) popular Vitamin Water. The only Coke-branded product to use stevia is the green-labelled Coca-Cola Life. First launched in South America in 2013 before being released elsewhere around the world, Coke＊s Life variety was developed as a reduced-calorie option in response to consumer demand for healthier alternatives. However, critics and consumers accused the Company of ＆greenwashing＊ (Han, 2015; Lee et al., 2017), and, like Vitamin Water before it, Coca-Cola Life＊s positioning as a healthy choice was disputed. This mixed reaction saw the product withdrawn from the majority of markets and largely discontinued. The current product evolution is still sold as Coca-Cola Life, though emphasis is placed on its inclusion of stevia (as a natural sweetener) and cane sugar (rather than controversial high fructose corn syrup). 
Branding ＆bloke Coke＊ to tap the male market 
Conscious that healthier choices were often marketed to women, Coca-Cola recognised the potential for a sugar-free cola that might appeal to men. Coca-Cola debuted Coke Zero in 2005 with the largest product launch since Diet Coke. However, consumers＊ initial responses were mixed despite strong dis-tribution and promotion. Pendergrast (2013, p. 439) suggested this was in part due to the vague original ＆real taste＊ campaign that failed to clearly convey Coke Zero＊s key point of di.erence. Any discourses of health and dieting were dropped in favour of championing ＆zero＊, yet in doing so lacked any emphasis that the product was sugar-free. The original white packaging also failed to di.erentiate it from Diet Coke＊s silver (the more masculine black pioneered in Australia and New Zealand was only adopted later). Coke Zero sought to create new (or reclaim lapsed) Coca-Cola drinkers rather than self-cannibalise from its existing market share. However, it su.ered from poor repeat purchases by taste-loyal consumers who were willing to try something new but unwilling to make a permanent switch. 
Coca-Cola persisted and made ground with mainstream consumers in 2007 after Coke Zero＊s ＆taste infringement＊ digital campaign went viral (Elliott, 2007). This was reinforced in 2009 with a spin-o. parodying Coke＊s iconic ＆Hey Kid, Catch!＊ commercial that was aired at the Super Bowl as well as promotional deals with James Cameron＊s record-breaking blockbuster Avatar. With a di.erent .avour pro.le (and di.erent blend of arti.cial sweeteners) to Diet Coke, Coke Zero picked up the ＆bloke Coke＊ identity and o.ered con-sumers a taste that was more closely aligned with classic, calorie-rich Coca-Cola. In order to further grow its following, Coke Zero was reformulated and rebranded over 2016每2017. It is now known as Coca-Cola Zero Sugar (or Coke No Sugar in some markets) to make the product＊s sugar-free qualities clearer to consumers. Whether the new consumer base has come at the expense of other Coke products remains unclear. 
Coca-Cola＊s global ＆one-brand＊ strategy in 2016 saw the four products in its Coke line 每 Coca-Cola, Diet Coke, Coke Zero, and Coke Life 每 brought under a single masthead for the .rst time and given a uni.ed ＆red disc＊ identity (Coca-Cola, 2019b). With more combined marketing (initially under the ＆taste the feeling＊ campaign), and ＆without creating di.erent personalities for its var-iants＊ (Roderick, 2016), this was a concerted e.ort to encourage brand loyalty regardless of consumer health, sugar, and taste preferences. Though the cam-paign was well received, Diet Coke e.ectively jumped ship some two years later. In an e.ort to rejuvenate the appeal of diet drinks in a shrinking market, and revive the brand, Diet Coke was given a major facelift with sleek new packaging, an updated logo and visual identity, and an extended line of .avours that were pitched as ＆more relatable and more authentic＊ for millennial con-sumers (Acevedo, cited in Wiener-Bronner, 2018). Ritson (2018) argued Coca-Cola ＆might be .ogging a soon-to-be-dead horse, but nobody has .og-ged better or harder than the marketers at Coke＊. The rebrand created positive impressions and boosted Diet Coke sales volumes in the US and the UK (Fleming, 2018). However, given Coke Zero purchases also rose while regular Coke declined (Fleming, 2018), these results re.ect other contextual factors more so than marketing success. Notably, sugar-free beverage sales were pushed up at the expense of their sugar-laden counterparts as a direct result of the introduction of regional sugar taxes. 
While Coca-Cola＊s products have been out of step with ongoing concerns around obesity, and the growth of health, wellness, and clean eating trends, the brand has remained undiminished. A combination of this cultural change and government interventions such as sugar taxes have pushed up consumption for sugar-free alternatives at the expense of their sugar-sweetened counterparts. There is compelling evidence for the public health and economic bene.ts of taxing sugar-sweetened beverages (Roache & Gostin, 2017; Chaloupka et al., 2019). Yet it is also ironic that while such taxes aim to reduce sales of ＆unhealthy＊ sugary drinks, they have ignored the consumption of sugar-free diet options that are arguably also unhealthy. Such mechanisms are important levers to ease demand in societies su.ering from the obesity epidemic and increased mortality from preventable diseases, though they are a single tool for a complex systemic problem. Other sustained behavioural change e.orts that address hol-istic dietetic and lifestyle factors are needed if the war on weight is to be won. 
Sugar-coating the science 
Coca-Cola＊s (2019a) frequently asked questions web page contains a large sec-tion devoted to addressing health issues and safety concerns associated with ＆reduced-and no-sugar substitutes＊. This feature acknowledges public anxieties while using yes/no and fact/.ction dichotomous language in an attempt to dismiss consumer fears. Signi.cantly, the term ＆arti.cial sweetener＊ is rarely used, replaced by the more innocuous ＆sugar substitute＊, to avoid any negative connotations. The overly simpli.ed and generalised statements are unlikely to build trust or satisfy consumers seeking evidence to inform their decision making. However, Coca-Cola has routinely sought to reframe discourse around carbonated soft drinks, such as by ＆referring to them instead as ※spark-ling beverages§ and insisting its arti.cially sweetened options be classi.ed as health and wellness products＊ (Pendergrast, 2013, p. 447). 
The arti.cial sweeteners used in sugar-free ＆diet＊ products have di.ered over time and between locations, to account for changes in consumer tastes and in line with government regulations. Originally sweetened with saccharin, Diet Coke was blended with aspartame once it was approved for use in the US in 1983. This approval was almost 20 years in the making, as the chemical underwent extensive testing (in light of the cyclamate/saccharin scare) before being deemed safe for use. Aspartame was also introduced to the TaB formula at the same time, which riled TaB fans who missed the strong kerosene kick that came from unmasked saccharin (Belkin, 1984). Diet Coke continues to use aspartame as its primary sweetener. In contrast, Coke Zero uses a blend of aspartame and acesulfame potassium (Ace K). However, each product＊s formula is type (can, bottle, fountain) speci.c and has a regional blend that may also include sucralose, cyclamate, saccharin, and stevia. Table 14.1 lists the sugar substitutes used in arti.cially sweetened Coca-Cola products, and summarises the main health concerns associated with each type. 
Carcinogenicity has been the most publicised anxiety associated with these chemicals. Throughout the 1970s and 1980s, questions were raised about the scienti.c rigour and bias of the original studies damning arti.cial sweeteners, as much of the early research was sponsored by the sugar industry (Donovan, 2014, p. 173每6). Eventual scienti.c consensus indicated there was no clear link between arti.cial sweeteners and cancer in humans (Priebe & Kau.man, 1980; Kroger et al. 2006; Lohner et al., 2017; Warner, 2011), yet it took several decades for restrictions around cyclamate and saccharin to change. The repu-tational damage of sugar substitutes has lasted even longer. Fluctuations in research .ndings not only created inconsistencies in the way arti.cial sweet-eners are regulated, but paranoid perceptions have persisted around the world. The myth that arti.cial sweeteners cause cancer is perpetually embedded in health discourse, as this information continues to be circulated by health advocates and the media despite evidence to the contrary. 
Research and publication bias is a serious and contentious issue for nutrition and health science. Nestle (2018) points out this con.ict of interest is di.cult to avoid given much research is industry funded. Systematic reviews of research bias on the e.ects of arti.cially sweetened beverages found: industry-sponsored studies were more likely to have favourable results, studies by competitor industries (such as sugar) reported unfavourable conclusions, and non-industry studies were mixed or inconclusive (Shankar et al., 2013; Mandrioli et al., 2016). The way in which research .ndings are dismissed or distorted to suit corporate interests is a signi.cant impediment for consumers seeking to make informed choices. 
More recently, connections have been drawn between arti.cial sweeteners and metabolic impairment, including the development of metabolic syndrome (Nettleton et al., 2009; Liauchonak et al., 2019). Metabolic syndrome (also called Syndrome X) describes a cluster of conditions that increase an indivi-dual＊s risk of cardiovascular disease and Type 2 diabetes. Thus, the diet industry is faced with the paradoxical proposition that arti.cial sweeteners may exacer-bate the same health issues as the sugars they are substituting. However, these studies are mostly observational or correlational, and while this is useful for identifying population-wide mortalities, it is problematic in establishing causa-tion (Romo-Romo et al., 2016; Gardner, 2014). For example: are arti.cially sweetened beverages harmful to your health, or do regular consumers lead otherwise unhealthy lifestyles? Stereotypes of overweight consumers drinking Diet Coke while chowing down on junk or fast food have been consistently applied as part of such discussions to shift responsibility from the product＊s nutritional value to the consumer and their personal lifestyle choices. Here Diet Coke is not the problem, because the self-indulgent consumer can be blamed instead. Hence Coca-Cola can abrogate accountability and argue that its zero-nutritional value products can still be enjoyed in moderation. 
Coca-Cola weighs in on the obesity war 
As Pendergrast (2013, p. 475) notes, many of ＆humanity＊s worst problems are self-in.icted＊. With the obesity epidemic expanding across the Western world, there has been considerable tension regarding cause and accountability. The pri-mary factors are generally thought to be a combination of sedentary lifestyles, portion size, and the promotion, convenience, and consumption of energy-dense, nutritionally poor foods (Lustig, 2013; Wiss et al., 2018). Food and beverage industry powerhouses like the American Beverage Association, Coca-Cola, and PepsiCo have all lobbied against, found ways around, or been forced to comply with anti-obesity strategies such as ceasing marketing to children, size restrictions, labelling, and sugar taxes (Bittman, 2010). At the same time, Coca-Cola and others have endeavoured to publicly excuse their products through health-oriented cor-porate social responsibility activities, such as sports partnerships and philanthropic donations to health and wellness programmes. 
Coca-Cola has occasionally used more explicit advertising to directly respond to health concerns, though this has typically been cynically received. In 2013, the Company ran its ＆Coming Together＊ campaign in the US and UK to showcase its low-and no-calorie beverage choices and frame its supposed lea-dership on smaller portion sizes and community activities as public health initiatives. Coca-Cola said it wanted to be more transparent about the calorie content of its products and help ＆to .nd meaningful solutions＊ to obesity (Coca-Cola, 2013). In contrast, Nestle (2013) argues the commercials were ＆an astonishing act of chutzpah, explainable only as an act of desperation to do something about the company＊s declining sales＊. The campaign drew compar-isons with Big Tobacco＊s advertising for low-tar cigarettes in the 1970s and 1980s and prompted ＆honest ad＊ parodies that called Coke out for being disingenuous (Stewart, 2013). 
＆Coming Together＊ was a very public e.ort to shift health discourse and be seen to play the part of a good corporate citizen. However, more signi.cant was Coca-Cola＊s role in the Global Energy Balance Network (GEBN). Estab-lished in 2014, the GEBN was a non-pro.t foundation, originally proposed by Coca-Cola, and led by professors from the University of Colorado and the University of South Carolina (O＊Connor, 2015; Ruskin, 2015). Its main agenda was to scienti.cally, publicly, and popularly advance the importance of ＆energy balance＊ (where high levels of exercise combined with high caloric intake) as the preferred framework to address the obesity crisis (O＊Connor, 2015). The multi-million-dollar initiative was administered through an orche-strated grants system to obscure the connection to the Coca-Cola Company, and published research funded by the GEBN also failed to disclose this con.ict of interest (Ruskin, 2015). In 2015, following an investigation by the New York Times (see O＊Connor, 2015) and the Associated Press, Coca-Cola was accused of astrotur.ng. These revelations and the public scrutiny that followed prompted a number of resignations and caused the GEBN to be disbanded in 2015, though not without igniting public debate about research bias and corporate accountability. 
The Company＊s internal documents revealed the GEBN was described as a ＆weapon＊ that could be deliberately portrayed as a ＆credible＊, ＆independent＊, and ＆honest broker＊ in the ＆growing war between the public health community and private industry＊ (Barlow et al., 2018). Opposing stakeholders have long sought to pin the blame for the obesity epidemic on any target other than themselves. For Coca-Cola, this meant actively changing the conversation by moving the focus away from nutrition and diet, particularly the need to reduce soft drink consumption (Barlow et al., 2018). The GEBN was used as a front group to fund research and advocacy programmes that emphasised the importance of exercise and physical activity to battle obesity, rather than nutritious diets and calorie control. These solutions are not mutually exclusive. Sedentary lifestyles are problematic, though excess caloric consumption and poor nutrition are greater contributors to weight gain (Je.ery & Harnack, 2007; Chantal et al., 2018). Public health advocates have strongly argued for increased physical activity in combination with a nutritious diet to maintain a healthy weight and address obesity (Fontaine et al., 2013). Spinning the science in this way is unethical and erodes public trust in subject matter experts. 
While these revelations destroyed the credibility of the GEBN studies, they also seriously undermined consumer con.dence in the transparency and inten-tions of scienti.c research and public health advocacy. Again, parallels were drawn between Coca-Cola＊s approach and that of Big Tobacco＊s ＆merchants of doubt＊, yet the Company quietly weathered the storm and dismissed such comparisons as ＆extreme＊ and ＆unreasonable＊ (Barlow et al., 2018). More damage was likely done to the integrity and reputation of the academic com-munity, while Coke went largely unscathed in the wake of the scandal. As for consumers, they were left as confused as ever when it comes to who and what to trust with regard to their health. 






Conclusion Health discourse has in.uenced, and been in.uenced by, Coca-Cola＊s brand, products, and corporate activities throughout history. The innovations of arti-.cial sweeteners and diet drinks have played a major part in this legacy. Although there is very little nutritional di.erence across Coke＊s diet colas, they do vary in terms of target market, brand identity, and selected sugar substitute (as well as taste, to the discerning consumer). The safety of these sweeteners continues to be contested, despite evidence that many accusations are unfoun-ded or overstated. Given this, consumer fears can be assuaged, though Nestle＊s (2018, p. 11) ＆personal food rule＊ to ＆never eat anything arti.cial＊ is still sage advice. It is also timely in the current cultural context where people are seeking more natural health options, which has challenged Coca-Cola to rethink and reframe its beverages and messaging. As with seemingly all nutrition and health science research, there are two warring sides: self-serving industry-funded studies whose results .nd in their favour; and independent studies that deliver more diverse conclusions. Con.icts of interest, as well as oppositional inter-industry competition between sugar and arti.cial sweeteners, are usually .agged by stakeholders or picked up by health media (Karasu, 2018). Though this is hindered by the food and beverage industry＊s lack of transparency and accountability (Nestle, 2018). As such, consumers must exercise their own judgement around their dietary choices until there is more conclusive evidence (or speci.c advice from their healthcare professionals). Coca-Cola advocates for the inclusion of its products as part of a balanced diet, and while this may be disingenuous (since limiting consumption limits sales, whereas encouraging sales is to encourage consumption), it is not neces-sarily factually incorrect. The prudent choice is of course to follow Nestle＊s (2018) recommendation to avoid consumption of arti.cial products in so far as it is possible. That wider public discourse and cultural norms are also moving in this direction is all for the good. However, if sweetened beverages are your vice, then picking between full-sugar and sugar-free options 每 given neither are ＆good＊ (i.e. nutritious) for you 每 is perhaps less important for general health than following standard dietary guidelines and the rule of all consumption in moderation that keeps these treats to a minimum. As Diet Coke＊s advertising suggested in 2002, we should simply ＆do what feels good＊ while remembering its slogan from 1998 that ＆you are what you drink＊. 




15 Coke and the competitor 
Andrew McCowan 
Coca-Cola has always been a leading brand 每 setting the standards and context for others to follow. It was the .rst mass distribution cola soft drink brand in the United States, and in many other markets around the world. The emo-tional and symbolic signi.cance of Coca-Cola in American culture was underlined by Kansas newspaper editor William Allen White, the ＆voice of middle America＊, who was pro.led in Life magazine for his 70th birthday in 1938, with a photograph showing him sipping a Coke at a soda fountain. He wrote that Coca-Cola is: ＆a sublimated essence of all that America stands for, a decent thing, honestly made, universally distributed, conscientiously improved with the years＊ (Pendergrast, 1993, p. 198). 
A generation later, American business journalist, Thomas Oliver, who grew up in Coke＊s home state of Georgia, summed up that ＆Coke had grown up with twentieth-century America, where rites of passage are marked by moving from sipping Coke as a soda pop to mixing it with rum as an adults＊ elixir＊.He goes on to enthuse that Coke is: ＆more than a drink: it is sandlot baseball, high-school pep rallies, that .rst driver＊s license, hot rods and street dances＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 4). 
In other words, Coke was a brand that was used to writing the rules as the de.nitive brand of cola soft drinks in the US. That put it above the fray of competitive battles with other soft drinks. Competitors would compare them-selves to Coke 每 but the comparisons were never made the other way around. Coca-Cola was the original and ＆the real thing＊. Even Roger Erico, CEO of Pepsi-Cola USA during the ＆cola wars＊ of the 1980s, summarised that: ＆drink a Coke and you were transfusing yourself 每 taking in a little bit of the soul of America. Coke was the American Dream in a bottle＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 5). 
It is interesting to consider that Pepsi is the one brand that managed to get under Coke＊s skin. Pepsi-Cola had been the perennial second-string brand, the alternative cola which Coke largely ignored. It was launched onto the market some 13 years after Coca-Cola and as a result of ＆some astute decisions by Coke executives, and some bad ones by Pepsi＊s owners [circumstances] combined to give Coke such a commanding lead that, until recently, Coke never mentioned Pepsi by name＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 17). However, as this chapter will demonstrate, Pepsi has played an integral role in the development of the Coke brand as well as The Coca-Cola Company. While Coca-Cola dismissed its competitor and saw itself as the ＆real thing＊, the spectre of Pepsi was always there, challenging Coke＊s claims and pushing it to work harder. 
*** 
The Coca-Cola ＆system＊ of bottlers and distribution deals had developed to put a Coke within ＆an arm＊s reach of desire＊ of all Americans and ensured that Coca-Cola was more readily available in more places than Pepsi, or any other soft drink brand. This dominance of the Coca-Cola system was such that by the late 1970s, Pepsi-Cola head of creative services, Alan Potash, has said: ＆The word Coke had practically become generic. And that gave us the problem that we＊ve been chipping away at ever since＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 41). 
However, during the 1970s the Coca-Cola system in the USA faced a series of legal and organisational challenges that distracted company executives, including marketing, so that they were: ＆bickering amongst themselves, dis-tracted by tangential issues＊, because they were ＆dodging government allega-tions [anti-trust battle with the Federal Trade Commission [FTC] over whether franchise agreements were illegal monopolies], arguing with bottlers over the price of syrup, and squabbling over whether or not to control who owned the company franchises＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 26). 
Oliver cites Donald R. Keough, the president of Coca-Cola USA, as saying of this period: ＆our system was immobilised＊ to the point where he reports that the .rst 50 meetings he attended after assuming o.ce as division president in 1974 were ＆legal brie.ngs on the FTC battle＊ (1987, p. 27). In his uno.cial history of The Coca-Cola Company, business writer Mark Pendergrast summed up that: ＆the approaching eighties found Coca-Cola in disarray＊ (1993, 
p. 327). 
This period of introversion and disarray was an opportunity for Pepsi-Cola as it ＆kept its own cola sharply in focus＊ so that with ＆its product more generally available, Pepsi-Cola in 1975 pulled ahead of Coca-Cola in supermarket sales＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 28). It also focused Potash＊s thinking about Pepsi＊s target audience for advertising, as Pepsi reframed the brand to: ＆position ourselves with those who had not yet developed this generic association. It forced us to look at the next generation of consumers as the only ones who might not have to rectify their behaviour, their attitudes towards colas＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 41). 
The targeting of a new generation was the .rst in a series of Pepsi marketing initiatives in the United States market which cumulatively undermined con-.dence among Coca-Cola executives and their intangible pride in the de.ni-tive appeal of Coke as the real thing (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 3). This was despite the apparent con.dence being asserted by the brand with its new advertising line ＆Coke is It!＊ which had been introduced in February 1982 and reasserted that Coke is a de.nitive symbol of identity and lifestyle aspirations for Americans. Goizueta is quoted as saying to a group of bottlers that ＆Coke is it!＊,as it ＆mirrors the nature of Americans today. We say what we mean and we tell it like it is＊ (Pendergrast, 1993, pp. 345每6). 
The steps taken by the Pepsi-Cola system included successfully increasing retail distribution in the US market, although the Coca-Cola system main-tained its strength of distribution through the soda fountain outlets (fast food, cinemas, and various other counter service venues). This greater availability was backed by the introduction of the ＆Pepsi Generation＊ advertising concept. With this idea, Pepsi-Cola was made into a ＆necktie product＊. The point of this approach, developed by Pepsi＊s agency BBDO, was that the necktie: ＆doesn＊t make the buyer feel good about the manufacturer of the tie; it makes him feel good about himself＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 86). Under this strategy the brand was expressing that: ＆what you drank said something about who you were＊. Alan Potash summarised this as creating: ＆an image of our consumer as active, vital and young at heart＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 41). 
The momentum was then reinforced by the Pepsi Challenge campaign. This campaign evolved from a local, tactical advertisement developed by a Pepsi bottler in the Dallas market that ＆brought research to life＊ and was: ＆disarmingly honest in its revelation. It showed that consumers, even lifelong Coke drinkers preferred the taste of Pepsi＊ (Dusenberry, 2005, p. 74). This was the genesis of a national campaign that, for .ve years, made ＆taste tests the centrepiece of Pepsi＊s advertising＊ and as such was a ＆declaration of war against Coke＊ (Dusenberry, 2005, p. 75). It caused immediate shock at Coca-Cola 每 especially after they conducted their own taste tests that con.rmed the results. Coke had never before ＆tested its lead drink against any competitive product＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 45). 
Although the Pepsi Challenge had a positive impact on Pepsi-Cola sales, it didn＊t directly switch consumers away from Coke. Rather it: ＆took away＃ from the shares of the other soft drink producers, as the beverage industry developed into a two-horse race＊ (Oliver, 1987, pp. 49每50). Nonetheless, the Challenge had a psychological victory over Coca-Cola, as one leading Coke bottler put it: ＆the pride of the company and bottlers was wounded＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 50). 
However, while the campaign successfully raised questions about the taste appeal of Coca-Cola, and successfully set up Pepsi as a true equal of Coke, the Pepsi Challenge advertising failed to ＆build on [Pepsi＊s] positives＊ as a ＆young company with new ideas＊ and contrast it with the old fashioned ＆Americana＊, which people associated with Coke (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 85). Addi-tionally, Coca-Cola had developed an advertising counter to the Challenge under the ＆Coke is It!＊ campaign 每 using comedian Bill Cosby (at the height of his fame and appeal, known as a long-term Coke fan, and well before sub-sequent controversies) to mock and ridicule the Pepsi Challenge by ＆directly addressing the audience, mugging with his well-rehearsed charm＊ (Pendergrast, 1993, p. 346). 
So, heading into the summer of 1984, Pepsi brought the Pepsi Challenge to an end while ramping up spending and star appeal with a series of commercials in The Choice of a New Generation campaign over 1984每1986. This advertising returned to the emotional territory of the Pepsi Generation, as ＆[it] celebrates Pepsi as the soft drink for young-thinking contemporary people＊, with advertising that ＆would exalt the user rather than sanctify the product＊ (Dusenberry, 2005, pp. 220每1). This was advertising that came to symbolise the era of American pop culture and connected to the sense that times were changing, and people needed to change their favourite soft drink to be part of it. 
The campaign famously used Michael Jackson, and later Lionel Richie, to advertise the Pepsi-Cola brand and included commercials that teased Coca-Cola and its belief in being ＆the real thing＊. This included one advertisement where an alien space ship beams Pepsi and Coke vending machines aboard before returning the Coke one and .ying o., described by Enrico as ＆the best damn Pepsi Challenge commercial ever dreamed up＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 87). It also included the one advertisement: ＆set in the future, [show-ing] an archaeology professor leading a class through a ※dig§ in a once-typical suburban home＊, and as ＆the students drink Pepsi＊ they examine various items unearthed, including an ＆old Coke bottle＊ which the professor examines but can＊t identify (Dusenberry, 2005, pp. 236每7). 
This campaign helped to establish that Coke was a brand of the past, old-fashioned, and lacking in the energy and excitement that Pepsi was beginning to o.er 每 and where the brands were available head to head, Pepsi was win-ning. The sheer muscle of the Coca-Cola system meant that the brands didn＊t compete head to head everywhere and so Coke retained its overall lead, despite Pepsi being well established as the number one cola in US supermarket sales. This fed the process that led to the people of Coca-Cola doubting their .agship product, so that by 23 April 1985, Pepsi had prodded Coca-Cola into under-mining its greatest strength 每 its very de.nitiveness, with the launch of the reformulated Coca-Cola recipe as ＆New Coke＊. At the time, Pepsi-Cola CEO Roger Enrico memorably wrote: ＆After 87 years of going at it eyeball to eye-ball, the other guy just blinked＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 200). 
This action has been labelled as a major ＆marketing blunder＊ and a ＆.asco＊ and is ＆widely regarded as one of the biggest marketing failures of all times＊ (Taylor, 2019). Enrico labelled it as ＆the world class blunder that was New Coke＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 3). It is indisputable that the launch of New Coke didn＊t work out as intended by The Coca-Cola Company. How-ever, the period of 79 days when New Coke was in market alone has been argued, at least within The Coca-Cola Company, as being a time: ＆that revo-lutionised the soft drink industry, transformed The Coca-Cola Company and stands today as testimony to the power of taking intelligent risks, even when they don＊t quite work as intended＊ (The Coca-Cola Company, 2012). This view may seem to be self-serving from the Coca-Cola system, but Beverage Digest, the independent trade journal, reported that: ＆Coca-Cola＊s share of the US soft drink market grew 6 percent in 1985 to 39 percent, the biggest position the company has held in decades＊ (Kilman & Smith, 1986). 
Some of the share growth achieved by the Coca-Cola system in the New Coke year can be attributed to the introduction of successful new products like Cherry Coke, along with the ＆explosive sales performance of Coke Classic [after reintroduction] 每 which [by February, 1986] outsold New Coke .ve to one, according to some bottlers＊ estimates＊ (Kilman & Smith, 1986). Coca-Cola had received an outpouring of support from the American public when they announced on 11 July 1985 that the original formulation would be reintro-duced as Coca-Cola Classic. Coca-Cola USA President Donald Keough said at the press conference: ＆the passion for original Coca-Cola caught us by sur-prise. ＃ It is a wonderful American mystery, a lovely American enigma, and you cannot measure it any more that you can measure love, pride or patriotism＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 180). 
Initially Coca-Cola continued to support the Coca-Cola brand in advertis-ing, featuring the new Coke product and the slogan ＆Coke is It!＊, although this was soon replaced by new, experimental slogans ＆The Real Choice＊ and ＆We＊ve got a taste for you＊. However, none of these really connected with the public. Towards the end of 1985, support for the Coca-Cola brand was sus-pended, until in early 1986 when Coca-Cola introduced a ＆two-tier advertising campaign＊ in the US market. This enabled the system to support having two Coca-Colas side by side in the market, by developing a separate and distinctive image for New Coke. Under this strategy, Coca-Cola Classic was presented as ＆a piece of the American landscape＊ using the ＆patriotic vignettes featuring ordinary＊ Americans as well as athletes and entertainers and the slogan: ＆Red, White and You＊ playing on the Coca-Cola red and white iconography, while New Coke would ＆wrestle with Pepsi-Cola＊ using a ＆hip and irreverent [style] of promotion＊ making use of a ＆Catch the Wave＊ slogan (Kilman & Smith, 1986). 
Irrespective of the impact in the marketplace and in popular culture more broadly, the 1985 launch of New Coke was a key moment in the Cola Wars when the Coca-Cola brand appeared to cave in to the competitive pressure. It went from being an entertaining marketing battle between equals to being one where: ＆the boys in Atlanta threw in the towel＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 13). In making the fateful decision to reformulate, Coca-Cola overlooked three critical points: 
1 Underestimating the emotional relationship with ＆the real thing＊ versus the 
rational relationship with the taste. In conducting the research into the 
New Coke formula, Coca-Cola＊s team overlooked the symbolic meaning 
of their product and treated it as ＆simply a thirst-quenching liquid 每 period＊ 
(Gelb & Gelb, 1986). 2 Not explaining to research respondents that the New Coke .avour would 
replace ＆the real thing＊. The result of this was, in the words of Ira Hebert, a 
marketing leader at Coca-Cola that: ＆we failed to tell the tasters graphically 
enough that their preference for the new would mean they would never 
be able to taste the original Coke again＊ (Gelb & Gelb, 1986). 
3  Forgetting the symbolic value of the action in the context of the Cola Wars. Enrico saw that the Cola Wars were good for both Pepsi and Coke as ＆consumer interest swells the market. The more fun we provide, the more people will by our products 每 all our products＊.He adds, ＆The catch is, the Cola Wars must be fun＊. The problem was, in Enrico＊s view at least, that Coke didn＊t see the competition with the same light-hearted spirit, and so the funny and cheeky advertising from Pepsi that tweaked the image of Coke left them ＆very, very peeved＊ (Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 12). 
Seventy-nine days after launching New Coke, Coca-Cola relented and reintroduced the original formula to the US market as Coca-Cola Classic. Nielsen sales data depicted that, just after the reintroduction of the original formulation, the combined market share of the two variants was: ＆exactly equal to Coke＊s market share before the expensive and embarrassing introduction of a new formulation＊ (Gelb & Gelb, 1986). 
However, in the fallout from the launch of New Coke, Coca-Cola Chair-man Roberto Goizueta introduced a new concept in brand marketing 每 the megabrand. Instead of one product being ＆the real thing＊, Coca-Cola would become the brand name for a family of products, and so they needed to ＆change the way that success is measured＊. This was because of the risk that both Coke and Coke Classic would ＆split Coke＊s share of the market, and Pepsi would look like a runaway winner even if the combined Coke products were walloping Pepsi＊ (Greising, 1998, p. 137). 
Arguably, for Goizueta ＆Megabrand was a concept of convenience＊, in that it was only a concept he talked about: ＆for so long as there was a risk that Pepsi would take the lead in all sales categories as New Coke and Coke Classic split shares＊. Little was heard from Goizueta about the megabrand concept ＆after Coke Classic regained the [US] market share lead in 1987＊ (Greising, 1998, p. 138). Nonetheless, the DNA of the Coca-Cola brand had been changed 每 instead of having a single ＆t＊ product, ＆the real thing＊, the precedent had been set that there could be two .avours worthy of the name ＆Coca-Cola＊, as well as other family .avours like Diet Coke and Cherry Coke. It took several years for the brand to sort out how the variants related to one another 每 eventually Coca-Cola evolved into a version of the megabrand concept introduced expediently at the end of the New Coke era. 
Despite the consumer backlash against its introduction, Coca-Cola＊s leader-ship retained their faith in the appeal of the new formula. So while Goizueta said ＆we have heard you＊ to disgruntled customers who wanted the original formula back, when he announced the launch of Coca-Cola Classic, he remained convinced of a: ＆simple truth 每 New Coke ＃ tasted better than the old version＊ (Pendergrast, 1993, pp. 360每5). 
However, Coca-Cola Classic surged in sales and eclipsed New Coke, in spite of the ＆Catch the Wave＊ advertising, and in the spring of 1986 McDonald＊s: ＆the largest dispenser of Coke, switched its 7,000 restaurants to Classic from New Coke＊ (Oliver, 1987, p. 188). New Coke never recovered from this and was clearly relegated to being, at best, a secondary element of the overall brand. Regardless of this, Goizuieta and his management＊s faith was such that .ve years after the introduction of New Coke they attempted to relaunch the for-mula under the name Coke II in test markets in Spokane, Washington and Chicago, Illinois. In both markets Coke II managed to snag around a 2.5 per cent share of the market which was ＆respectable, but not enough to give Coke II legs for a national roll-out＊(Greising, 1998, pp. 199每200). 
The megabrand approach has evolved in the decades since the New Coke introduction but now means that the ＆Coca-Cola＊brand represents four core var-iants, each with a di.erent sweetener and therefore a di.erent taste 每Coke Classic with Sugar, Coke with No Sugar, Coke with Stevia, and Diet Coke (with arti.cial sweeteners and a lighter taste). There are various .avours and ca.eine versions of these core variants. This preponderance of ＆new＊Coke products in the market reveals just how far The Coca-Cola Company had come. Of course, Coke was not alone in expanding its o.erings 每Pepsi had similarly developed a suite of products and variants. 
*** 
During the Cola Wars, and especially when it provoked the launch of New Coke, Pepsi-Cola had also demonstrated that the great Coca-Cola system could be needled and distracted by a feisty challenger. So, Pepsi has continued needling Coke as a challenger cola brand. It is still the smaller challenger brand globally, and still trails in the US market: ＆Last year, dollar sales of regular Coke in the US increased 1 percent, while regular Pepsi fell 2 percent, according to Beverage Digest＊ (Schultz, 2019). 
The spirit of the Cola Wars is to create, in the words of Roger Enrico: ＆some innocent fun＊with Pepsi being ＆the scrappy underdogs in the industry＊(Enrico & Kornbluth, 1986, p. 12). Over the years since the launch of New Coke, the Cola Wars have been conducted around the world, as the two great rivals battled over dominance of the carbonated soft drinks category. Each has had successes and failures in that time, but the rivalry has endured. 
Pepsi can still act as the cheeky challenger, as they were for the Super Bowl of 2018, when for the .rst time, Pepsi ＆invaded Atlanta＊每running advertising in the hometown of The Coca-Cola Company in its role as the o.cial soft drink of the NFL. Bloomberg news reported: ＆Pepsi is having fun crashing Atlanta＊s love fest with Coke. Messages like ※Look who＊s in town for Super Bowl LIII§poke fun at its rival from downtown billboards, subway stations and garbage cans＊(Sasso & Williams, 2019). 
Coca-Cola is also continuing to play its part in the ongoing cola wars 每the fallout of the New Coke launch had ＆turned the gigantic marketing blunder into a commercial coup＊as ＆the venerable cola roared back to claim its lead as the premier American soft drink (Pendergrast, 1993, p. 370). This is testament to the enduring potency of the Coca-Cola brand, even as the company had ＆misread its customers and risked the future of the world＊s most successful 



Coke and the competitor 233 brand＊. In essence, management were lucky that ＆Coca-Cola was strong enough to save itself＊ (Greising, 1998, p. 138). Ironically, Coca-Cola＊s latest marketing move in 2019 was reintroducing the New Coke formulation as a limited time o.er in the US in association with the Net.ix programme Stranger Things to gain attention of a new generation of Cola Wars fans and provoke more interest (Taylor, 2019). The launch of New Coke was a seminal moment in American popular cul-ture and speaks to the ongoing fascination with the Cola Wars and the moves and counter-moves taken by the two great rivals as well as the unique place the Coca-Cola brand has at the heart of American identity.
